Teacher Resources 226


**Grade 2 Unit 10|**
##### Introduction


This introduction includes the necessary background information to be used in teaching the
_Fighting for a Cause_ unit. The Teacher Guide for _Fighting for a Cause_ contains fourteen daily lessons,
each of which is composed of two distinct parts, so that the lesson may be divided into smaller
chunks of time and presented at different intervals during the day. Each entire lesson will require a
total of sixty minutes.


This unit includes two Pausing Points following Lesson 6 and Lesson 12. At the end of the unit, a
total of four days, with one day each for Unit Review and Culminating Activities and two days for
the Unit Assessment, are included to allow time to review, reinforce, assess, and remediate content
knowledge. You should spend no more than twenty-one days total on this unit.


~~**TEACHER RESOURCES**~~


At the back of this Teacher Guide, you will find a section titled “Teacher Resources.” In this section
you will find the following:


  - Free Verse Poetry Rubric


  - Activity Book Answer Key


  - Measures of Text Complexity


  - Texas Essential Knowledge and Skills Correlation Chart


  - English Language Proficiency Standards Correlation Chart


~~**UNIT COMPONENTS**~~


Along with this Teacher Guide, you will need:


  - Flip Book for _Fighting for a Cause_


  - Image Cards for _Fighting for a Cause_


  - Activity Book for _Fighting for a Cause_


  - Digital Components for _Fighting for a Cause_


All unit components’ materials can also be found on the program’s online materials.


Introduction
**1**


~~**WHY FIGHTING FOR A CAUSE IS IMPORTANT**~~


This unit will introduce students to several ordinary people who stood up for what they believed
in and who fought for a cause, even when faced with immeasurable odds. They will learn about
some key figures who fought for various causes, such as religious freedom, the abolition of slavery,
and the equal protection of the law for all people. Each of these individuals were parts of broader
movements that helped change many laws in their respective countries. Students will understand
the connection between ideas and actions and how ordinary people can do extraordinary things.


The _Fighting for a Cause_ unit will introduce students to nine leaders:


    - Queen Esther


     - William Penn


     - William Wilberforce


     - Jackie Robinson


     - Rosa Parks


     - Martin Luther King Jr.


    - Cesar Chavez


     - Dolores Huerta


     - Dr. Héctor P. García


Students will learn about the dedication and sacrifice of these individuals, as well as the significant
impact they had on the fight for civil rights and human rights. This unit will also introduce students
to four young people who fought for a cause—Kelvin Doe, Mikaila Ulmer, Sydney Etzel, and Lauren
Anderson.


The texts that students will be reading and discussing in this unit provide opportunities to build
content knowledge and draw connections to social studies. You may build on class discussions to
support students in making cross-curricular connections to the strands of Citizenship, History, and
Social Studies Skills from the Social Studies TEKS. This content is not a replacement for grade-level
social studies instruction. For more information on religious source material in reading/language
arts, consult the program guide. For more information about the quantitative and qualitative
measures used to determine the complexity of the texts, see the Measures of Text Complexity in the
Teacher Resources.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause
**2**


~~**WHAT STUDENTS HAVE ALREADY LEARNED**~~


The following units, and the specific core content that was targeted in those units, are particularly
relevant to the Read-Alouds students will hear in _Fighting for a Cause:_


**• America: Our Great Country (Kindergarten)**


**• American Independence (Grade 1)**


Introduction
**3**


~~**CORE VOCABULARY FOR FIGHTING FOR A CAUSE**~~


The following list contains all of the core vocabulary words in _Fighting for a Cause_ in the forms in
which they appear in the Read-Alouds or, in some instances, in the “Introducing the Read-Aloud”
section at the beginning of the lesson. Bold-faced words in the list have an associated Word Work
activity. The inclusion of the words on this list does not mean that students are immediately
expected to be able to use all of these words on their own. However, through repeated exposure
throughout the lessons, they should acquire a good understanding of most of these words and begin
to use some of them in conversation.

Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause
**4**


~~**CORE CONTENT OBJECTIVES**~~


  - Describe how movements such as the _abolitionist movement_ and _civil rights movement_ helped
change laws and improve the lives of many people.


  - Explain why fighting for important causes has helped to change laws and improve the lives of many
people.


  - Explain the terms _discrimination_ and _segregation._


  - Explain the concepts of _nonviolence_ and _pacifism._


  - Describe the lives and contributions of Queen Esther, William Penn, William Wilberforce, Jackie
Robinson, Rosa Parks, Martin Luther King Jr., Cesar Chavez, Dolores Huerta, and Dr. Héctor P. García.


  - Identify the main causes for which Queen Esther, William Penn, William Wilberforce, Jackie Robinson,
Rosa Parks, Martin Luther King Jr., Cesar Chavez, Dolores Huerta, and Dr. Héctor P. García fought
during their lifetimes.


  - Describe the similarities among the causes supported by Queen Esther, William Penn, William
Wilberforce, Jackie Robinson, Rosa Parks, Martin Luther King Jr., Cesar Chavez, Dolores Huerta, and
Dr. Héctor P. García.


  - Describe the similarities among the methods of _nonviolence_ used by Queen Esther, William Penn,
William Wilberforce, Jackie Robinson, Rosa Parks, Martin Luther King Jr., Cesar Chavez, Dolores
Huerta, and Dr. Héctor P. García.


~~**WRITING**~~


In this unit, students will write free verse poetry in which they express their opinions (Lessons 2–11).
They will then revise, edit, and publish one of the free verse poems (Lesson 12). During the editing
process, students will practice editing drafts for prepositions and prepositional phrases. These poems
may be added to students’ writing portfolios to showcase student writing within and across units.


**Cursive Writing**


In Grade 2, students participating in the skills units have been learning to form cursive letters and


develop their cursive writing. Students are encouraged throughout this knowledge unit to use their


cursive skills to complete activity pages. ~~**TEKS 2.2.E**~~


**TEKS 2.2.E** Develop handwriting by accurately forming all cursive letters using appropriate strokes when connecting letters.

Lesson 1 Change through Non-Violence
**7**


**8**



~~**ADVANCE PREPARATION**~~


**Application**

 - Prepare and display a Central Idea/Supporting Evidence Web similar to the
one that follows. Alternatively, you can find the digital version in the online
materials for this unit.


 - Prepare to model a free verse poem using the one provided in the lesson or
one created in advance.


~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**abolish, v.** to get rid of

Example: Many people worked hard to abolish slavery around the world.
Variation(s): abolished


**civil rights, n.** rights that provide equal protection under the law to all
citizens

Example: Martin Luther King Jr. wanted civil rights, such as the right to vote,
for all people.
Variation(s): civil right


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**courage, n** . bravery when facing danger or a difficult situation

Example: It took a great deal of courage to stand up against laws that
were unfair.
Variation(s): none


**discrimination, n.** unfair treatment of a person, or group of people, because
of their skin color, race, or religion

Example: As an African American, Jackie Robinson experienced
discrimination when he was unable to eat at the same restaurant as his
white teammates.
Variation(s): none


**movement, n.** a series of events involving a large group of people working to
accomplish something, typically a social change

Example: Martin Luther King Jr. is one of the most well-known leaders of the
civil rights movement.
Variation(s): none


**nonviolence, n.** an attempt to peacefully correct an unfair situation without
using physical violence

Example: Many people who worked to ensure the laws of our country
provided equal protection to all groups of people did so through
nonviolence.
Variation(s): none

**Lesson 1: Change through Non-Violence**
##### Introducing the Read-Aloud



10M



**10**



**Language:** Students will collaboratively review the various meanings of the words
_fighting_ and _cause_ .


~~**TEKS 2.1.D; TEKS 2.6.E**~~


~~**CORE CONNECTIONS (5 MIN.)**~~


  - Explain that this unit is called “Fighting for a Cause.” Ask students what they
think this title may mean.


Check for Understanding


**Brainstorming:** Ask students to work with a partner to create
a definition for the word fighting and a definition for the word
cause. Then ask them to work together to develop an idea about
the topic of the unit. Before students begin, use each term in a
sentence. For example, “Angelica was fighting hard to finish the
race in first place.” ~~**TEKS 2.1.D**~~


Have several students share their responses with the class.
_(Answers may vary.)_


  - Explain that the word _fighting_ as used in the title of this unit does not mean
using fists or physically hitting another person.


  - Here, the word _fighting_ means working hard to accomplish something.


  - Explain that a cause is a goal. So, “fighting for a cause” means working hard
to reach a goal.


  - Tell students that for the next couple of weeks, they will learn about several

when recognized, making appropriate contributions, and building on the ideas of others; **TEKS 2.6.E** Make connections to
personal experiences, ideas in other texts, and society.

ordinary people who helped make extraordinary changes in the United States

~~**IMAGE PREVIEW (5 MIN.)**~~


 - Explain to students that, prior to each Read-Aloud, they will be introduced to
these people through an Image Preview activity. In the first lesson, students
will see an image of each of the individuals mentioned in this Read-Aloud.


**Show Image 1A-3: Queen Esther**

 - Explain that this is a picture of Queen Esther, a woman who saved the Jewish
people living in Persia.


**Show Image 1A-4: William Penn**

 - Explain that this is a picture of William Penn, who fought for all people to have
religious freedom.


**Show Image 1A-5: William Wilberforce**

 - Explain that this is a picture of William Wilberforce, who fought to end slavery
in the British Empire.


**Show Image 1A-7: Jackie Robinson**

 - Explain that this man is Jackie Robinson, and he worked to make it possible
for African Americans to play professional sports in the United States and
Canada.


**Show Image 1A-8: Rosa Parks**

 - Explain that this woman, Rosa Parks, helped make life fairer and better for
African Americans living in the American South in the 1950s and 1960s by
courageously challenging an unfair practice.


**Show Image 1A-9: Martin Luther King Jr.**

 - Explain that this is Martin Luther King Jr., and he did many things to make
life better for all Americans. Ask students if they have ever heard of Martin
Luther King Jr. If they do not recognize his name, remind them that there is
a special day set aside each year in January when our whole country takes
time to remember the wonderful things Martin Luther King Jr. accomplished
during his lifetime.


**Show Image 1A-10: Cesar Chavez and Dolores Huerta**

 - Explain that these people are Cesar Chavez and Dolores Huerta. They saw
that some farm workers were not fairly paid for the work that they did.
Explain that Chavez and Huerta helped organize people to change this
situation.

**Language:** Students will demonstrate an understanding of the Tier 2 word _courage_ .


~~**PURPOSE FOR LISTENING**~~


  - Tell students to listen carefully for the reasons the author gives for the
statement that the people in this unit “made the world a much fairer place.”


~~**“PEOPLE WHO FOUGHT FOR A CAUSE” (15 MIN.)**~~


**Show Image 1A-1: People asking to be heard**

Throughout history, many courageous

individuals have taken a stand for an

important cause. They have stood up for the

freedom to worship as their faith directs them,

for the freedom to live and work where they

choose, and for freedoms protected by **civil**

**rights** . ~~_Civil rights are rights granted by law to_~~

~~_ensure the freedoms we have are available to every citizen of our country. In_~~

~~_our country, the Declaration of Independence describes our unalienable rights_~~

~~_as coming from our Creator, and the Constitution provides the framework_~~

~~_for these rights granted by law._~~ Many people have had to fight hard to

secure these rights. There are many different ways to fight peacefully for a

cause. Some ways include giving speeches, writing books or poetry, talking

to leaders to convince them to change certain laws, providing business

opportunities, or refusing to buy certain things. These kinds of actions can all

be part of fighting for a cause.


**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen activity, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses;
**TEKS 2.6.A** Establish purpose for reading assigned and self-selected texts; **TEKS 2.6.E** Make connections to personal
experiences, ideas in other texts, and society; **TEKS 2.7.B** Write brief comments on literary or informational texts that
demonstrate an understanding of the text; **TEKS 2.7.F** Respond using newly acquired vocabulary as appropriate.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


Over the next couple of weeks, you will hear eight stories from history about

people who took a stand for their beliefs. You will discover that most of these

people lived a long time ago. Even though the people you will read about used

nonviolence to “fight” for their causes, they often did so at great personal

risk. ~~_Great personal risk means that they showed bravery by standing up_~~

~~_for their cause, even when it meant putting themselves in harm’s way._~~ Even

though the stories come from a long time ago, what these people achieved

still impacts us today. They made it possible for us to have many of the

freedoms we have today.


**Show Image 1A-2: Religious freedom**

First, you will read about people who fought

for religious freedom. This is the freedom to

practice religion without the government telling

you how. You might remember from the _Land_

_of Opportunity_ unit that the right to freedom of

religion is guaranteed by the Bill of Rights in the

U.S. Constitution and that many immigrants

moved to the United States for this reason.


**Show Image 1A-3: Queen Esther**

You will read a story from the Bible about

Queen Esther, a Jewish woman who was

chosen by the king of Persia to be queen. An

advisor convinced the king to destroy the

Jews, but the king did not know this would

include his own queen. You will read about

what Queen Esther did to fight for her people.


**Show Image 1A-4: William Penn**

You will also read about a man named William

Penn who was sent to jail in England multiple

times because of his religion. He practiced

a different religion than the official Church

of England, and he was punished for having

different religious beliefs. William Penn

founded the colony of Pennsylvania, and he

made sure it was free from religious **discrimination** and a place where the

have done to fight for
her people.

what William Penn did
to make the world a
much fairer place.

**Show Image 1A-5: William Wilberforce**

Next, you will read about people who fought

for freedom to work and live by ending slavery.

You will hear the story of William Wilberforce,

a British politician born in 1759. He became a

devout Christian, and, because of his beliefs,

he became part of the abolitionist **movement** .

~~_A movement happens when a large group of_~~

~~_people come together to support a cause._~~ Wilberforce continued to work to

pass laws against slavery even after his own life was threatened because of

his beliefs.


**Show Image 1A-6: Civil rights**

You will read the stories of people who fought

for freedoms provided by civil rights under

the law. You’ll learn that throughout history,

in other countries, and in older times in our

country, some people had freedoms, but

others did not. People who fought for civil

rights worked to make sure that the laws and

freedoms available in our country were given to all U.S. citizens. Civil rights

in the United States include freedom of speech, freedom of religion, the right

to vote, and protection from **discrimination** . Today in the United States,

there cannot be a law that gives rights to only certain people. But long before

you were born, that was not the case. And many people fought to make our

country better.


**Show Image 1A-7: Jackie Robinson**

You’ll learn about Jackie Robinson, a great

baseball player who fought to ensure people

could not be discriminated against in

professional sports based on their skin color.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Show Image 1A-8: Rosa Parks**

You’ll learn about Rosa Parks, a woman who

fought to ensure that people could not be

discriminated against because of their skin

color when using a bus or any other kind of

service. She helped organize the Montgomery

Bus Boycott, where people refused to pay to

ride the bus until the discrimination stopped.

This boycott helped grow the civil rights movement, which involved actions

taken by many people across the country that resulted in changes to ensure

that our country’s laws apply equally to everyone.


**Show Image 1A-9: Martin Luther King Jr.**

Martin Luther King Jr. is perhaps the most

famous leader of the civil rights movement.

He believed in bringing about change through

**nonviolence** . ~~_Nonviolence is a refusal to_~~

~~_take physical action against someone who is_~~

~~_treating people badly or unfairly._~~ Because of

King’s leadership, the Civil Rights Movement

forced change and helped create opportunities for many people who had

experienced discrimination.


**Show Image 1A-10: Cesar Chavez and**
**Dolores Huerta**

Others followed the model set by Martin

Luther King Jr. and worked to extend civil

rights even further. Cesar Chavez and Dolores

Huerta fought to ensure that laws provided

equal protection to farmworkers in California,

who were often of Hispanic origin.



Support

fairer place.

Support

and Dolores Huerta
did to make the world
a much fairer place.

**16**



**Show Image 1A-11: Dr. Héctor P. García**

After serving as a hero in World War II, Héctor

P. García also worked in Texas to ensure

that the laws of our country provided equal

protection to Hispanic Americans.


Many of the people in the stories you will

hear had to fight to make sure that freedom was given to all, not just some.

Today, it’s easy for us to take these freedoms for granted. ~~_To take something_~~

~~_for granted means to not think about or appreciate something, but just to_~~

~~_assume you’ve always had it._~~ It’s important to recognize and remember

the achievements of the people before us so we understand what these

important people did for our nation and the world. The people you will learn

about had courage. ~~_What does courage mean?_~~ Sometimes these people

were bullied or threatened, but this did not stop them from doing what they

knew was right. As you hear these stories, think about how different life was

all those years ago when these people set out to make the world a better

place. When we’re through, you’ll even hear about some kids today who are

continuing to work to improve our world.


~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


**1. Literal.** What does it mean to fight for a cause? _(To support and work hard_
_for something you believe in)_


**2. Literal.** What is discrimination? _(Unfair treatment of a person, or group of_
_people, because of their skin color, race, or religion)_


**3. Inferential.** What are some of the civil rights guaranteed by the United
States? _(Some of the civil rights guaranteed by the United States are_
_freedom of speech, freedom of religion, the right to vote, protection from_
_discrimination, and equal protection under the law.)_


**4. Inferential.** Why is it important for us to remember the achievements of the
people in the Read-Aloud? _(Answers may vary but should include that without_
_these people standing up and fighting for these causes, we would not have the_
_rights that we have today. Life might be very different for us.)_

Check for Understanding


**Identify the Individual:** Explain that there will be certain words
or phrases written on eight sticky notes and ask students to take
turns placing each sticky note on the image of the person whose
accomplishment is described on the note.


- This person is perhaps the most famous member of the civil
rights movement and believed in bringing about change through
nonviolence. _(Martin Luther King Jr.)_


- This person was a queen who saved the Jewish people living in
Persia. _(Queen Esther)_


- This person worked to make it possible for African Americans
to play professional sports in the United States and Canada.
_(Jackie Robinson)_


- This person started the Montgomery Bus Boycott, which helped
grow the civil rights movement. _(Rosa Parks)_


- These people worked to ensure that laws provided equal
protection to farmworkers in California. _(Cesar Chavez and_
_Dolores Huerta)_


- This person worked to end slavery in the British Empire. _(William_
_Wilberforce)_


- This person worked to ensure that there was religious freedom
in the early American colonies. _(William Penn)_


- This person fought to ensure the laws of our country provided
equal protection for Hispanic Americans. _(Dr. Héctor P. García)_

Lesson 1 Change through Non-Violence
**17**


**18**



~~**WORD WORK: COURAGE (5 MIN.)**~~


1. In the Read-Aloud you heard, “The people you will learn about had courage.”


2. Say the word _courage_ with me.


3. Courage is bravery when facing danger or a difficult situation.


4. This time, John knew that he had found the courage he needed to climb
the mountain.


5. If you had to find the courage to do something, what would it be? Share your
thoughts with the other students in the class. Try to use the word _courage_
when you tell about it. [Ask two or three students. If necessary, guide and/or
rephrase the students’ responses: “I would need courage to . . .”]


6. What’s the word we’ve been talking about?


**Use a Making Choices activity for follow-up.** Explain that you are going to
read several sentences describing situations that might require a person to
have courage. If students think a person would need courage in a situation,
they should say, “That would take courage.” If they think that a person would
not need courage, they should say, “That would not take courage.”


   - I will learn to ride a bike. _(That would take courage.)_


   - I will play tag with friends. _(That would not take courage.)_


   - I will go to the dentist. _(That would take courage.)_


   - I will take a test. _(That would take courage.)_


   - I will go on vacation. _(That would not take courage.)_


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Lesson 1: Change through Non-Violence**
##### Application


**Writing:** Students will study the structure and form of free verse poetry and will
learn how to synthesize information from the Read-Aloud while preparing to write
their own free verse poem.


~~**TEKS 2.6.H; TEKS 2.9.B; TEKS 2.9.D.i**~~


~~**WRITING FREE VERSE (15 MIN.)**~~ ~~**TEKS 2.6.H**~~


  - Ask students what impressed them most about each of the individuals
discussed in this Read-Aloud.


  - Have students share what they felt were the challenges faced by these
individuals.


  - Explain that students will be writing free verse poems about each of the
people in this unit, beginning with the next lesson, where they will learn about
Queen Esther.


  - Explain that there are no rules about how to write free verse poems; free
verse poems do not have to rhyme, and there can be as many or as few
words on a line as the poet wishes.


  - Explain that free verse poems are simply made up of the words the poet
chooses to write. In addition, free verse poems can form shapes. Students
can accomplish this by placing a different number of words on each line so
that the outline of the poem forms a shape.


  - Explain that students first need to plan their poem by brainstorming ideas.


  - Model the brainstorming process that students will use when planning their
free verse poems by directing their attention to the Central Idea/Supporting
Evidence Web prepared earlier.


  - Ask students to think about the points raised during the Read-Aloud (e.g.,
civil rights, how these people worked to make the world a fairer place, etc.).
Record this information on the Central Idea/Supporting Evidence Web.


**TEKS 2.6.H** Synthesize information to create new understanding; **TEKS 2.9.B** Explain visual patterns and structures in a
variety of poems; **TEKS 2.9.D.i** Recognize characteristics and structures of informational text, including the central idea and
supporting evidence with adult assistance.



Support


Review some of the
important facts students
have learned about the
people discussed in
this lesson. Use images
from the Read-Aloud as
necessary.



Lesson 1 Change through Non-Violence
**19**


**20**



Check for Understanding


**Recall:** Ask students to explain what a free verse poem is.
_(It does not have to rhyme; there can be as many or as few words_
_on a line as the poet wishes; and it can form different shapes,_

_depending on how many words are placed on each line.)_


 - Model how to turn these ideas and details into a free verse poem, either by
using one you created earlier, or by using the one below:


_No more worries, or tears,_


_Or sighs,_


_When we say—CHANGE is coming NOW._


 - Explain that the words, phrases, and rhythm used in the poem can all be used
to express the student’s opinion.


~~**VOCABULARY INSTRUCTIONAL ACTIVITY (5 MIN.)**~~


**Word Work: Achievement**

 - Remind students that they heard in the Read-Aloud, “It’s important to
recognize and remember the achievements of the people before us so we
understand what these important people did for our nation and the world.”


 - Have students say the word _achievement_ with you.


 - Explain that an _achievement_ is something gained through hard work.


 - Give the following example: _Getting an “A” on Mrs. Smith’s math test is a real_
_achievement._


 - Have students think of someone who is known for a particular achievement.
Have two or three students share their responses and, if necessary, guide
and/or rephrase their answers: “____’s achievement is (was). . .”


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Use a Making Choices activity for follow-up.** Provide the following
examples and have students say either “That is an achievement” or “That is
not an achievement.”


    - I stubbed my toe. _(That is not an achievement.)_


    - I read an entire chapter book by myself for the first time. _(That is an_
_achievement.)_


    - Louis ate a cookie. _(That is not an achievement.)_


    - Taylor did not complete her homework. _(That is not an achievement.)_


    - Even though it was a little scary, Maya gave a speech to the class about
why she wanted to be the class president. _(That is an achievement.)_


**End Lesson**

Lesson 2 Esther Part 1: Becoming Queen
**23**


**24**



~~**ADVANCE PREPARATION**~~


**Speaking and Listening**

 - Display Timeline (Digital Component 2.1).


 - Display Character Chart (Digital Component 2.2).


 - Display Image Card 1 (map of the Persian Empire).


**Note to Teacher**
Digital Component 2.1 (Timeline) is for use during this lesson only. The
timeline used during the Application section will be used over the course of
the unit and kept up in the classroom.


**Reading**

 - You will need a map of the world or a globe to show where the United States
is today and where Iran is today, which is where the Persian Empire was
located.


 - Students who participated in the program in Kindergarten will remember
learning about King Solomon. Earlier in Grade 2, students learned about
Ancient Greece, the battle between Greece and Persia at Thermopylae, and
the differences between presidents and kings. Students who participated
in the program in Kindergarten and Grade 1 may also remember that early
American colonists and Founding Fathers wanted to make sure America was
a place of religious freedom.


**Application**

 - Create and display a Fighting for a Cause Timeline large enough to
accommodate nine image cards.


~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**authority, n.** the ability or power to make rules and decisions

Example: The king has the authority to decide what rules are needed.
Variation(s): none


**empire, n.** the large grouping of territories or countries under the control of
a single supreme ruler

Example: King Xerxes’s empire extended from Ethiopia to India.
Variation(s): none


**fasted, v.** did not eat for a period of time, sometimes for religious reasons

Example: David fasted as he prayed for the life of his son.
Variation(s): fast, fasting


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**perish, v.** to become destroyed or ruined; to cease to exist; to die

Example: The war caused people to perish.
Variation(s): perished


**plot, n.** a plan to cause trouble

Example: The boys made a plot to stay up past their bedtime.
Variation(s): plots

##### Introducing the Read-Aloud



10M



**EMERGENT**
**BILINGUAL**
**STUDENTS**


Speaking and Listening


Speaking Opinions

**Beginning**
Reframe questions as
simple yes/no questions
(e.g., “Is a president
elected to serve for a
specific period of time?”).


**Intermediate**
Provide students with a
specific sentence frame
(e.g. “Presidents have less
power because . . .”).


**Advanced/**
**Advanced High**
Encourage students
to use key details in
complete sentences (e.g.,
“One difference between
kings and presidents is
that kings serve for life
and presidents serve for
terms.”).


~~**ELPS 2.G; ELPS 2.I**~~


**26**



**Speaking and Listening:** Students will listen actively and answer questions using
multi-word responses.


~~**TEKS 2.1.A**~~


~~**WHAT HAVE WE ALREADY LEARNED? (5 MIN.)**~~


  - Remind students that they learned in the _Land of Opportunity_ unit that many
people immigrated to America because they wanted religious freedom. In
some countries around the world, today and throughout history, people
can be punished if they practice a religion in a way that is not approved by
the government. In today’s story, you will hear about someone who wanted
to make sure her religious group was free to practice their religion without
being harmed by her country’s government.


  - Tell students that, in today’s story, there is a king. Ask students the following
questions to review what they have learned:


    - Who can remember some of the differences between kings and
presidents? _(Kings serve for life, and presidents serve for terms. A president_
_is elected to the position, and kings are not.)_


    - Who usually has more power, a king or a president? _(A king has more_
_power than a president.)_


  - Explain to students that today’s story is set in ancient Persia. Ask students
if they remember learning about ancient Persia. Remind students that,
earlier in the year, they learned in Unit 2: _Ancient Greeks: Life, Tradition, and_
_Government_ that ancient Persia attacked Greece. Remind students that one
of the Persian kings they learned about was Xerxes. Explain that there were
several ancient kings that used the name Xerxes. They will also hear the
name Xerxes in today’s story. Some believe the Xerxes in today’s story is the
same Xerxes you learned about, but others do not.


  - Background for Teacher: The story of Esther was first written in Hebrew, and
is part of both the Hebrew and Christian Bibles. In various translations from
the original Hebrew, the name of the king is translated as Xerxes, Ahasuerus,
and Achashverosh. Some students may be familiar with the story of Esther,
and may know the king by those other names.


**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen actively, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


~~**ESSENTIAL BACKGROUND KNOWLEDGE (5 MIN.)**~~


  - Explain to students that the story today comes from the Hebrew Bible, which
is also organized as the Old Testament of the Christian Bible. Members of the
Jewish and Christian religions believe that key stories of the Hebrew Bible tell
the history of the Jewish people.


  - Today’s story comes from a book of the Hebrew Bible and Christian Bible
entitled _Esther_, and it is the story of one person who had faith and performed
a brave deed that saved an entire group of people.


**Setting**

  - Show students the timeline on Digital Component 2.1. Use the timeline to
help students understand when this story took place. [Key events on the
timeline include the signing of the Declaration of Independence, the voyage
of the _Mayflower_, the Civil War, the Battle of Thermopylae, and the reign of
King Solomon.] Explain to students that this story took place around the
same time as the Battle of Thermopylae. [Esther is added to the timeline
later in the lesson.]


  - Using a world map or globe, help students locate the country of Iran. Tell
students that Persia and Iran are two names for the same country. Iran is the
official name, but Persia was used in history, and some of the people of Iran
today are referred to as Persians.


  - Display Image Card 1 that shows the Persian Empire. Explain to students that
the story they will listen to today is set in the city of Susa in ancient Persia.
While this is an ancient city, it still exists today in modern-day Iran. Have
students locate Susa on the map. Use a map of the world or globe to show
where Persia would be today and where the United States is located.

~~**IMAGE PREVIEW (5 MIN.)**~~


 - Display the Character Chart (Digital Component 2.1).


   - Refer to this chart throughout the lesson.


 - Explain to students that the characters they will encounter include:


   - Esther—an orphaned young woman living Persia, far from the homeland of
her people


   - Xerxes— the king of Persia


   - Mordecai—Esther’s cousin, who served as her guardian; Esther was an
orphan, so Mordecai raised her.


   - Haman—an evil, scheming man who attained a high position in the king’s
court

Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Lesson 2: Esther Part 1: Becoming Queen**
##### Read-Aloud


**Reading:** Students will evaluate details to determine key ideas.


~~**TEKS 2.1.A; TEKS 2.6.A; TEKS 2.6.G; TEKS 2.7.C**~~


**Language:** Students will demonstrate an understanding of the phrase “give
credit.”

~~**TEKS 2.7.F**~~


~~**PURPOSE FOR LISTENING**~~



30M




  - Listen carefully to evaluate details and determine key ideas about the story
of Esther.


~~**“ESTHER: GUARDIAN OF HER PEOPLE” (15 MIN.)**~~


**Show Image 2A-1: Map of the Persian Empire**

The story of Esther begins about two thousand

five hundred years ago, in the year 483 BC,

in the kingdom of Persia. The Persian Empire

was ruled by King Xerxes [/zerk*seez/]. At

the time, people living in Persia had diverse

religious beliefs and backgrounds. Jews were

one of these groups. Some of them settled in Persia after having been forced

to leave their homeland when it had been invaded and destroyed years

before. Persians and Jews had different customs. They dressed, spoke, and

worshiped differently. One of these Jewish people was a young, orphaned

girl named Esther. Esther lived in the Persian city of Susa with her cousin

Mordecai [/mord-duh-kai/], who raised her. ~~_How do you think Esther might_~~

~~_feel living in the Persian city of Susa?_~~


**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen actively, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses;
**TEKS 2.6.A** Establish purpose for reading assigned and self-selected texts; **TEKS 2.6.G** Evaluate details read to determine
key ideas; **TEKS 2.7.C** Use text evidence to support an appropriate response; **TEKS 2.7.F** Respond using newly acquired
vocabulary as appropriate.



Support


Refer to the timeline on
Digital Component 2.1 to
help students keep track
of the time period.


Support


Explain to students that
the term _guardian_ in the
title means a protector.
Later in the story, Esther
will be in a position to be a
_guardian_ for her people.


Challenge


Have students make an
inference about Esther

**30**



**Show Image 2A-2: Esther**

When Esther was a young woman, King Xerxes

decided to find a wife to serve as his queen.

When he met Esther, he decided that she was

the one, so he married her and made her the

queen of the **empire** . ~~_An empire is the territory_~~

~~_or land under control of a ruler._~~ Now, Esther

had not revealed her identity, because Mordecai

had forbidden her to do so. The king did not know that Esther was Jewish.


Even after Esther was made queen, Mordecai still checked on her. “Every day

[Mordecai] walked back and forth near the courtyard . . . to find out how Esther was

and what was happening to her.” [1] ~~_Why would Mordecai want to stay near to Esther?_~~


**Show Image 2A-3: Mordecai standing near**
**the gate**

As time went by, Mordecai stayed near the

gate, the place where all the business of the

city was conducted. He would send messages

to Esther through the palace officials that were

Esther’s helpers. Those messages reminded

her to keep her beliefs a secret in order to

stay safe. Esther “continued to follow Mordecai’s instructions as she had

done when he was bringing her up.” [2] ~~_Why would Esther continue to follow_~~

~~_Mordecai’s instructions, even though she was no longer living with him?_~~


**Show Image 2A-4: Mordecai overhears the**
**plot**

One day, as Mordecai was sitting at the king’s

gate, two of the king’s officers became angry

with the king. They were so angry that they

made a plan to kill King Xerxes. Mordecai

overheard the **plot** . ~~_A plot means a plan to_~~

~~_cause trouble._~~ He told Queen Esther what he

had heard. Queen Esther reported this information to the king, giving Mordecai

the credit. The two officials were punished, and the king’s helper wrote the

story down in his official book of records. Queen Esther and Mordecai had

saved the king. ~~_What does it mean to give someone credit for something?_~~



1 Esther 2:11
2 Esther 2:20



Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Show Image 2A-5: Haman and King Xerxes**

Sometime after these events, King Xerxes gave

a man named Haman a very important position.

Haman had been given **authority**, ~~_or power to_~~

~~_make decisions_~~, to advise the king on important

things that impacted the entire country of Persia.

As a person of authority, the king commanded

everyone to bow down to Haman, and to bow with

special honor. Mordecai refused to bow with special honor. When Haman learned

that Mordecai refused to bow with special honor to him, he became so angry that

he wanted to destroy Mordecai and all of the other Jewish people.


**Show Image 2A-6: A special ring**

Haman used his position of authority with

the king to put his plan into action. He tried

to convince the king that because the Jews

in Persia followed different traditions from

other people in the kingdom, they should be

persecuted. He argued that they did not follow

the king’s rules. He asked the king to make a law that would destroy the Jewish

people. The king agreed to Haman’s plan. Xerxes gave Haman a special ring

that gave Haman the authority to pass laws.


**Show Image 2A-7: Haman casting lots**

Haman decided to cast lots to decide which

month should be the one in which he would

destroy the Jews. “Casting lots” is a way to

leave something to chance, somewhat like

rolling dice. In ancient Persia, some people

made decisions based on this trick. This led

Haman to the decision that the twelfth month

would be the one in which he would carry out his plan to harm the Jews.


Haman then passed a law that said that on a certain day in the twelfth

month, all of the Jewish people living in King Xerxes’s empire would be

attacked, and he posted the law throughout Persia. In every part of the

country, the Jewish people mourned, wept, and fasted. Fasting means

choosing to go without food for a period of time. Like other Jewish people,

when Mordecai heard this news, he became quite sad. He went into the city,

wailing and crying near the king’s gate.


Lesson 2 Esther Part 1: Becoming Queen
**31**


**32**



**Show Image 2A-8: Mordecai shares his plan**

Esther had not yet heard the news of Haman’s

plan. When she learned about Mordecai’s sadness,

she sent a messenger to find out what was wrong.

She learned about the plan to hurt the Jewish

people. ~~_What would be the impact of Haman’s law_~~

~~_on Esther’s family, like her cousin Mordecai? Do_~~

~~_you think the law would apply to Esther?_~~ Mordecai

sent instructions to Esther to go to the king to plead for mercy for her people.

~~_To plead for mercy means to ask for a lesser punishment._~~ But this was no simple

request. In those days, the king of Persia had a strict law: anyone, including the

queen, who went to the king with a formal request without his invitation could be

put to death. ~~_How do you think Esther would feel about the idea of approaching the_~~

~~_king without permission?_~~ Esther did not immediately approach the king, instead

telling Mordecai of the risks. Mordecai sent another message to Esther. It said,

“Do not think that because you are in the king’s house you alone of all the Jews

will escape. For if you remain silent at this time, relief and deliverance for the Jews

will arise from another place, but you and your father’s family will **perish** . And who

knows but that you have come to your royal position for such a time as this?” [3]

~~_Deliverance means being rescued or set free. Perish means to cease to exist or_~~

~~_die. “For such a time as this” has become a common expression that means being_~~

~~_chosen for a special moment or event. What does Mordecai mean when he says_~~

~~_this to Esther?_~~

him to gather all of the Jews living in Susa,

the capital of the Persian empire, and they

in the Persian empire. ~~_What do you think will_~~

~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


**1. Inferential.** Why would Haman want to hurt all of the Jews because of
Mordecai‘s refusal to bow to him? _(Haman was too proud and wanted to be_
_seen as powerful; Haman did not like Jewish people and their customs.)_


**2. Literal.** What did the king have recorded into the history book? _(He recorded_
_that Mordecai had saved him from the two men who plotted against him.)_


**3. Literal.** What did Mordecai do when he heard that the Jewish people were to
be hurt? _(He went into the city, wailing and crying near the king’s gate.)_


**4. Inferential.** Why would the Jewish people fast? _(Fasting was part of their_
_religion. It showed their faithfulness to God.)_


**5. Inferential.** Why would Mordecai advise Esther to keep her Jewish heritage a
secret from the king? _(The Jewish people were not accepted by the kingdom;_
_Mordecai was unsure how the king would react when he found out that Esther_
_was Jewish.)_

Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**advisors, n.** people who help by giving advice

Example: The students met with their advisors for guidance on which
classes they should take.
Variation(s): advisor


**custom, n.** a well-known way of doing things according to society

Example: It is custom to have food during a celebration.
Variation(s): customs


**descendant, n.** a member of a family line

Example: She is a descendant of her grandmother and grandfather.
Variation(s): descendants


**mourning, n.** deep sadness following the loss of someone or something

Example: The man was in mourning when his wife passed away.
Variation(s): none


**parade, v.** being marched or shown in front of people to celebrate someone’s
accomplishments

Example: We wanted to parade him through the streets for all he had done
for the town.
Variation(s): paraded, parades


**Purim, n.**


1. the plural version of the Hebrew word pur, which means “lots” as in “casting
lots”


2. a Jewish celebration that involves giving gifts of food to friends, giving charity
to the poor, and eating a meal


Example: The Feast of Purim is a celebration that involves giving gifts of
food to friends, giving charity to the poor, eating a meal, and listening to the
story of Esther.
Variation(s): pur (Hebrew)


**reign, n.** a time when a ruler is in charge

Example: The king’s reign lasted for fifty years.

**Lesson 3: Esther Part 2: Guardian of Her People**
##### Introducing the Read-Aloud


**Speaking and Listening:** Students will review what they have learned about
Esther and the character traits she showed in standing up for her people.


~~**TEKS 2.1.A**~~


~~**WHAT HAVE WE ALREADY LEARNED? (5 MIN.)**~~


  - Remind students that they have learned that in history, many people have
been interested in religious freedom, or the ability to practice religion without
the government telling you how. Ask them to recollect yesterday’s lesson
when they started to read a story about Esther, who wanted to make sure
the Jewish people remained free without being attacked by their government
and neighbors.


  - Have students recall what they learned in the previous lesson. Remind
students that this is a story from the Bible. Use the following questions to
prompt discussion:


    - Who was Esther? _(Esther was an orphaned Jewish girl living a long time ago_
_in the country of Persia. She was raised by her cousin Mordecai, but she hid_
_her Jewish identity.)_


    - Who was King Xerxes? _(Xerxes was the king of Persia, who chose Esther to_
_be his wife.)_


    - What good deed did Mordecai do to help the King? _(At one point, Mordecai_
_discovered a plot to kill King Xerxes and reported it to the queen, who_
_stopped the plot. Mordecai’s good deed was recorded in the king’s official_
_book of records.)_


    - Who was Haman? _(A man in a position of power in the court. While Xerxes_
_was king, he promoted Haman to a position where Haman could make laws_
_for the entire country, and Haman expected people to bow to him.)_


    - How did Mordecai make Haman angry? _(He refused to bow with special_
_honor to Haman.)_


    - What did Haman do about this? _(Angry at this, Haman created a law to_
_have all the Jewish people in Persia destroyed. He cast lots to determine_
_when it would be—the twelfth month.)_


**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen actively, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses.

**42**




 - Mordecai was worried about the new law, so he spoke to Esther. What did he
want Esther to do? _(Mordecai asked Esther to speak to the king to try to stop_
_Haman’s plan from happening.)_


 - What would happen to Esther, or anyone, who went to the King with a request
without an invitation? _(If Esther or anyone else went to the king without an_
_invitation, the laws of ancient Persia meant that Esther could be put to death,_
_unless the king spared her life.)_


 - What character traits describe Esther in the story from the previous lesson?
_(kind, obedient, faithful, brave)_


~~**ESSENTIAL BACKGROUND INFORMATION OR TERMS (5 MIN.)**~~


 - Explain to students that, in today’s story, they will learn more about Esther’s
choice. She could choose to continue hiding her identity from the king,
hoping she would be spared from the destruction that Haman had planned,
or she could take a risk and reveal her true identity to the king. But she would
be risking her own death.


 - Students will also learn how the Jewish holiday of Purim began.


 - Explain that today’s story will tell of an attempt to kill all Jewish people.
Unfortunately, that has happened more than once in history.


 - Remind students that in the kindergarten Kings and Queens unit, they
learned about a king named Solomon. The story of King Solomon is also a
story from the Bible. What was King Solomon most remembered for? _(King_
_Solomon is most remembered for his wisdom.)_


 - Explain that wisdom is a trait of King Solomon. ~~_A trait is a characteristic that_~~
~~_someone has or possesses._~~ Explain to students that some traits exist outside
of their control. In the same way that they cannot control the outcome of
rolling the dice, they cannot control their eye color or their height. Other
traits, though, are characteristics that they can develop in themselves. For
instance, they can choose to be honest, kind, brave, or faithful.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


Check for Understanding


**Turn and Talk:** When we left off yesterday, Esther was forced to
make a choice. She could either go to the king and ask him to
stop Haman from hurting all of the Jews, or she could keep hiding
her identity and remain silent in order to protect herself, knowing
that all of the Jewish people would be hurt. But if she went to
the king to ask for help, she could be put to death. What do you
think Esther will choose to do? What emotions might she feel?
_(Answers may vary but students may express that Esther probably_
_felt afraid.)_

at the dinner. At once, the king agreed and

sent the invitation to Haman. Haman was excited to receive this invitation

**Show Image 3A-3: Mordecai paraded through**
**the city**

The night before the dinner, King Xerxes

could not fall asleep, so he asked for his book

of records. This book contained all major

events that had happened during his **reign**,

~~_or time when a ruler is in charge._~~ He read

about Mordecai’s actions that saved him from death. ~~_Do you remember what_~~

~~_Mordecai did?_~~ The king began to wonder if Mordecai had ever been rewarded

for his actions. When he found out that Mordecai had not been rewarded, he

instructed Haman to collect a royal robe and a horse and to **parade** Mordecai

through the streets of Susa. ~~_Sometimes we use parade as a noun, like when_~~

~~_we watch a 4th of July parade. In this case, parade is a verb, meaning to be_~~

~~_brought in front of people to celebrate someone’s accomplishments._~~ Haman

was angry but did as the king said. As Mordecai was paraded through the

streets, Haman proclaimed, “This is what is done for the man the king

delights to honor!” [2] This meant that people who the king liked would be

rewarded. ~~_How do you think Haman feels about having to honor Mordecai?_~~


2 Esther 6:11



Support

paragraph.


Challenge

“This is what is done for

_something right or helpful,_
_you will be rewarded.)_

Lesson 3 Esther Part 2: Guardian of Her People
**45**


Support

**Show Image 3A-4: Angry King Xerxes**

The day of the dinner arrived. As the three sat

and ate, King Xerxes asked Esther again, “What

is your petition? It will be given you. What is

your request? Even up to half the kingdom,

it will be granted.” [3] Esther answered the king

by saying, “If I have found favor with you, Your

Majesty, and if it pleases you, grant me my

life—this is my petition. And spare my people—this is my request. For I and

my people have been sold to be destroyed, killed and annihilated.” [4] ~~_Annihilate_~~

~~_means to destroy something._~~ King Xerxes became angry and asked, “Who is

he? Where is he—the man who has dared to do such a thing?” [5] ~~_Who is Queen_~~

~~_Esther referring to?_~~


**Show Image 3A-5: King Xerxes gives**
**Mordecai the ring**

Queen Esther turned, pointed at Haman, and

called him an evil man. Haman stood, terrified,

before the king and queen. The king decided

what would happen to Haman and punished

him by having him put to death. When the king

learned that Mordecai was related to Esther,

and given all that Mordecai had done for the king, Xerxes took the special

ring that had belonged to Haman and presented it to Mordecai. This made

Mordecai one of the king’s most trusted **advisors**, and he could now pass

laws like Haman did before. ~~_Advisors are people who help by giving advice._~~

Mordecai was also given all of Haman’s property and wealth. ~~_How do you_~~

~~_think this will affect the Jewish people in Persia? Do you think Mordecai will be_~~

~~_able to stop Haman’s order to attack the Jewish people?_~~


**Show Image 3A-6 Purim**

However, the order to attack the Jewish people

throughout the Persian Empire still stood.

According to Persian law, once an order was

stamped with the king’s ring, the rule was final.

Not even King Xerxes could undo it. The only

way to take back or undo the law was to create

a new one. Therefore, King Xerxes allowed Mordecai to issue a new law,

signed with the special ring, to warn all the Jews in the kingdom of Haman’s

wicked plan so that they could protect themselves from harm.


3 Esther 7:2
4 Esther 7: 3-4
5 Esther 7:5


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause



**46**


When the day came for Haman’s original law to be implemented, when the

Jewish people living in Persia were to be attacked and destroyed, Haman’s

plan ultimately failed. Instead of being destroyed, the Jewish people banded

together and defended themselves against those who wanted to harm

them. “Mordecai recorded these events, and he sent letters to all the Jews

throughout the [nation] of King Xerxes, near and far, to have them celebrate

annually . . . [during the twelfth month of the Hebrew calendar] as the time

when the Jews got relief from their enemies, and as the month when their

sorrow was turned into joy and their **mourning**, ~~_or deep sadness_~~, into a day

of celebration. [6] ” ~~_Remember when Haman had cast lots to decide when he_~~

~~_would carry out his plan? What number had been determined when Haman_~~

~~_had cast lots before? What was that game of chance called?_~~ These days of

celebration for the Jewish people were called **Purim**, based on the same

game of chance played by Haman. This day became a celebration to give

thanks to God, who had demonstrated that he, not chance, determined what

would happen to the Jewish people.


**Show Image 3A-7: Remembering Esther’s**
**bravery**

The Jewish people living in Persia decided

to “establish the **custom** that they and their

**descendants** and all who join them should

without fail observe these two days every year

. . . in every generation by every family, and …

in every city.” [7] ~~_A custom is a well-known way_~~

~~_of doing things according to society. Descendants are people who came from_~~

~~_someone else._~~ They wanted to make sure that the memory of these days

of **Purim** would “never fail to be celebrated by the Jews—nor should the

memory of these days die out among their descendants. [8] ” ~~_Purim is a Jewish_~~

~~_celebration that involves giving gifts of food to friends, giving charity to the_~~

~~_poor, and eating a meal. Why do you think Purim is such an important holiday_~~

~~_to Jewish people?_~~


Because of Mordecai and Esther’s willingness to stand up for their people

and their beliefs, they were able to save the Jewish people living in Persia.

They fought for what they knew was right and made a difference that not

only affected the Jews of Persia but also Jewish people today.

7 Esther 9:27–28

~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


**1. Inferential.** What can you learn from the story of Esther? _(Other human_
_beings are valuable and important. It is worth risking our own safety to stand_
_up for others. We can have faith that our life has a purpose and our choices_
_matter.)_


**2. Literal.** What did Mordecai say to Esther to help her make her decision to be
brave and help her people? _(She was not safe; she was in danger just like the_
_rest of her people.)_


**3. Literal.** What holiday is still celebrated today by Jewish people to remember
Esther’s bravery and faith? _(Purim)_


**4. Inferential.** How did a person or group of people face hardship or danger
to defend what is right in this story? ( _Mordecai risked angering Haman; the_
_Jewish people banded together and fasted in the face of persecution; Esther_
_had courage to approach the king even though she could be killed._ )


**5. Inferential.** How did Esther’s faithfulness help her to be brave? _(She drew on_
_the belief she placed in God and in Mordecai’s leadership to help her trust that_
_she could stand up for her people.)_


Exit Pass


Students will describe at least two character traits Esther showed
by standing up for her people and will include text evidence to
support them.

~~**WORD WORK: CUSTOM (5 MIN.)**~~


1. In the Read-Aloud you heard, “The Jewish people living in Persia decided to
“establish the custom that they and their descendants and all who join them
should without fail observe these two days every year . . . in every generation
by every family, and … in every city.’”


2. Say the word _custom_ with me.


3. A custom is a well-known way of doing things according to society.


4. Celebrating the holiday Purim is a custom in the Jewish culture.


5. Can you think of any other customs? Are there special customs that your
family has? _[Ask two or three students. If necessary, guide and/or rephrase_
_the students’ responses: “Another custom is . . .”]_


6. What’s the word we’ve been talking about?

on Fridays, etc.

~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**Puritans, n.** members of a Christian religious group who wanted to simplify
worship using the Bible as a guide and break away from the Church of
England

Variation(s): Puritan


**Quakers, n.** members of a religious group called the Religious Society of
Friends

Variation(s): Quaker


**jury, n.** a group of people selected to listen to evidence in a trial and decide
the result

Variation(s): juries


**colony, n.** a region or place ruled and controlled by a faraway country

Variation(s): colonies


**treaty, n.** a formal agreement between countries

Variation(s): treaties


**diverse, adj.** lots of variety, different

Example: The invention of the steam train changed the way midwestern
society functioned.
Variation(s): none

**Start Lesson**


**Lesson 4: William Penn: A Friend of Peace**
##### Introducing the Read-Aloud



10M



**54**



**Speaking and Listening:** Students will review how Esther showed bravery in
standing up for her people.


~~**TEKS 2.1.A**~~


~~**WHAT HAVE WE ALREADY LEARNED? (5 MIN.)**~~


  - Using the Fighting for a Cause Timeline, review the content studied thus far.


  - Have students identify the individual placed on the timeline. _(Queen Esther)_


  - Ask students to describe how Queen Esther showed bravery. _(She revealed_
_that she was Jewish, even though it meant that she could be killed, so that the_
_Jewish people living in Persia could be saved.)_


  - Ask students what lessons could we learn from the story of Queen Esther?
_(Answers may vary but could include to stand up for what you believe in, to be_
_brave in the face of danger, and to do the right thing even if it is scary._


  - Explain to students that the story we heard about Esther is set in ancient
Persia, a long time ago. Point to the timeline, and point to a map/globe for the
location of Iran where Persia was located. Explain where we live today, in the
United States. Point to the timeline, and point to a map/globe for the location
of the United States.


  - Explain to students that even though these places are very, very far away
from each other, people in both places want religious freedom. And even
though the story of Esther happened a long, long time ago, people in both
times wanted religious freedom.


  - Explain to students that today we are going back in time to the same time the
American colonies were being settled by the English. Point to the _Mayflower_
voyage on the timeline. Explain that this happened a long time ago, and a
long time after the story of Esther. But even during this time, people wanted
religious freedom.


**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen actively, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


~~**IMAGE PREVIEW (5 MIN.)**~~


 - Point to a map/globe, and show the location of England. Tell students that
today they will hear the story of a person who fought for religious freedom
in England. Remind students that in Lesson 1, they learned that the people
in this unit fought in nonviolent ways. The person they will learn about today
behaved in a way consistent with his faith, which stressed being peaceful and
nonviolent. He suffered consequences for his beliefs, though—he was even
put in jail. But because he fought for freedom many years ago in England,
we have religious freedom today in the United States. This man’s name was
William Penn.


**Show Image 4A-1: William Penn**

 - Tell students that today in the United States, people are free to practice
their religion without the risk that they will be punished by the government.
In ancient Persia, they did not have that kind of religious freedom because
Jewish people were almost attacked because of their religion. But in our
country, we have religious freedom.


 - Explain that one of the reasons people have religious freedom in the United
States today is because when our country was founded, it was founded
by many people who wanted religious freedom. Many people left England
because, in England, they did not have religious freedom. They could be
punished if they didn’t practice religion the way the government of England
required. Many of these early founders of our country wanted to make sure
that here in America, everyone had the freedom to worship as they believed
without the government telling us how.

the United States was
a country, it began
as thirteen English
colonies more than 300
years ago. We declared
our independence from
England.



Lesson 4 William Penn: A Friend of Peace
**55**


**Lesson 4: William Penn: A Friend of Peace**
##### Read-Aloud



30M



Support


Review the meaning of
_religious freedom_ .



**Reading:** Students will explain how William Penn advocated for religious freedom
and will ask and answer questions about the Read-Aloud to demonstrate
understanding.


~~**TEKS 2.1.A; TEKS 2.6.A; TEKS 2.7.C**~~


~~**PURPOSE FOR LISTENING (5 MIN.)**~~


  - Tell students to listen carefully to learn how William Penn stood firm in his
beliefs and created positive change for others.


~~**“WILLIAM PENN: A FRIEND OF PEACE” (15 MIN.)**~~


**Show Image 4A-1: William Penn**

The state of Pennsylvania was named after an

Englishman called William Penn who lived over

350 years ago. Penn believed that countries

should not have laws that dictate how people

worship. He was a believer in religious

freedom, ~~_which is the right to practice religion_~~

~~_without the government telling you how_~~ . He

was also devoted to the idea that people should be free to express their

opinions without being put in jail.


Penn was born in the busy city of London, England, in 1644. His father was Sir

William Penn, an important leader in the English navy. Penn grew up in a very

difficult time when there was a lot of debate in England about who should

lead the country, a king or representatives elected by the English people.

At this time, the country of England followed the teachings of the Church of

England.


**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen actively, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses;
**TEKS 2.6.A** Establish purpose for reading assigned and self-selected texts; **TEKS 2.7.C** Use text evidence to support an
appropriate response.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause



**56**


Check for Understanding


Why was it a difficult time in England when Penn lived there?
_(Answers may vary but may include that at that time, England was_
_having a lot of debate about who should lead the country and the_
_country followed the teachings of the Church of England.)_


**Show Image 4A-2: Puritans**

During this time, some people suffered because

they were treated differently due to their

religious beliefs. Groups like the Puritans often

faced discrimination. The **Puritans** were a group

of people who wanted to “purify” or change the

Church of England. They preferred simpler ways

of worshiping over what they considered to be

extravagant ceremonies. They wanted to practice religion in their own way using

the Christian Bible as a guide. But because they worshiped differently than the

official Church of England, many other people didn’t trust them.


**Show Image 4A-3: Quakers**

In those days, if you didn’t follow the Church

of England, you could get into big trouble.

You could be fined or put in jail. The Puritans

weren’t the only ones being treated this way.

Another Christian religious group called the

**Quakers** also faced tough times. Just like

the Puritans, the Quakers wanted to worship

in their own way without anyone telling them they were wrong. When

William Penn was a young student, he started to learn about the beliefs of

the Quakers. They did not follow the Church of England’s requirements for

worship such as using the Book of Common Prayer to guide services and

ceremonies. They believed that everyone was equal in the eyes of God, and

that fighting with violence wasn’t right. Because their ideas were different

from the Church of England, they sometimes got into trouble. The English

government even made rules against them. At times, it was illegal for

Quakers to be public officials or meet together to worship. When they broke

these rules, they had to pay fines or even go to jail.



Challenge


Tell students that
_extravagant_ means over
the top, exaggerated, or
very fancy. Tell students
that _extravagant_ and
_simple_ are antonyms or
opposites.



Lesson 4 William Penn: A Friend of Peace
**57**


Support


William Penn’s faith
inspired him to bring
about change even when it
landed him in jail and was
unpopular.


Challenge


William Penn refused to
take off his hat when he
went before the judge.
Remember that Mordecai
refused to bow before
Haman. How are these
actions similar, and how
are they different? What
was different about what
happened as a result?
_(A jury decided to set_
_Penn free, versus a king_
_approved Mordecai and his_
_people being destroyed)_


**58**



**Show Image 4A-4: Penn and the Quakers**

As William Penn learned more about the

Quakers, he felt a strong connection to their

Christian beliefs. Penn decided to become one

of them. Remember those rules against the

Quakers? Penn didn’t think those rules were

fair, and he wasn’t afraid to say so. He did not

fight with violence. His way of fighting for his beliefs was to speak publicly

about them. But standing up for what he believed wasn’t always easy. It

came with consequences. There were times when he was put in jail for

attending Quaker meetings or for speaking out against the unfair treatment

of Quakers. While Penn was in jail, he used his time to write about his beliefs.

He wrote a book called _No Cross, No Crown_ in 1668 from a jail cell in the

Tower of London. He was put there because he wouldn’t give up his Quaker

beliefs and spoke out for the freedom to choose any religion.


**Show Image 4A-5: The Church of England**

In 1670, the government of King Charles II in

England made a new rule. The new rule said

that no more than five people could meet

together unless they were worshiping in the

Church of England. This was a way to stop

groups like the Quakers. William Penn decided

to preach to crowds in the streets anyway. This

got him into trouble, and he was taken to court. When he was in court, he did

something surprising: he refused to take off his hat in front of the judge. To

him, this was a way of saying everyone was equal. The **jury**, ~~_a group of people_~~

~~_deciding if he was innocent or guilty_~~, agreed with Penn. They believed he had

the right to believe and speak what he wished and be treated with kindness.

And soon, Penn would get a chance to create a place where that dream could

come true.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Show Image 4A-6: A new place called**
**Pennsylvania**

In 1681, King Charles II gave William Penn a

large piece of land. ~~_Why would the king give_~~

~~_Penn valuable land?_~~ It turns out that the king

owed Penn’s father a big favor, and instead

of paying him back with money, the king gave

Penn land in North America. Penn saw this as a perfect chance to make a

special place for Quakers and anyone else who was treated unfairly because

of their beliefs. The new colony was named Pennsylvania in memory of

Penn’s father.


**Show Image 4A-7: From England to**
**Pennsylvania**

William Penn wanted his colony in North

America to provide basic freedoms. A **colony**

is a region or place ruled and controlled by a

faraway country. Penn wrote those freedoms

into the rules for his colony. Those rules were

Pennsylvania’s Frame of Government, which was written in 1682. It said

people in Pennsylvania had the freedom to worship God how they wanted,

without fear of being put in jail. It said that everyone would get a fair trial if

they got into trouble. And it said that people could vote for who they wanted

to represent them. Soon, many Quakers had crossed the Atlantic Ocean to

make a home in Pennsylvania. ~~_[On the map of England to Pennsylvania, point_~~

~~_out that England is where William Penn grew up and Pennsylvania is where he_~~

~~_moved too.]_~~ They weren’t the only ones, either. Many people decided they

liked the sound of William Penn’s promises. The Quakers strongly believed

that all people are equal in the eyes of God. They thought men and women

should be treated the same by the laws. They also believed in sharing the

land with the Native American tribes. These beliefs became part of the laws

of Pennsylvania.


**Show Image 4A-8: Life in Pennsylvania**

William Penn met with the Lenape and offered

to make a **treaty**, ~~_or agreement_~~, with them.

He treated them as equals and promised to

respect their lands. Penn believed in learning

from the Native Americans. They taught the

settlers about the land, how to grow crops,

William Penn helped

Pennsylvania and inspired
the principles of America
that we enjoy today.

Lesson 4 William Penn: A Friend of Peace
**59**


**60**



and how to survive the cold winters. In return, the settlers shared their own

knowledge and tools with the Native Americans. William Penn had a big

dream for Pennsylvania. He wanted it to be a special place where people

could live happily and be treated fairly, no matter what they believed. He

wanted a place where kindness, fairness, and respect ruled. Penn didn’t

keep this dream to himself. He wrote about it and made speeches about

it. Penn’s ideas inspired other Founding Fathers such as Thomas Jefferson

and Benjamin Franklin. Jefferson once said that Penn was the “greatest

lawgiver the world has produced.” Word quickly spread. People from different

places with different beliefs and different dreams all started to come to

Pennsylvania. This made Pennsylvania a very **diverse** place. This means

that there were people from different backgrounds and cultures all living

together. They learned from each other. They shared food, stories, traditions,

and the dream that William Penn had started.


Check for Understanding


In Unit 9, _Land of Opportunity_, you learned about how the United
States is very diverse. What was the United States referred to in
that lesson? _(A melting pot)_ How is the colony of Pennsylvania like a
“melting pot”? _(Answers may vary but could include that there were_
_many different people with different beliefs living there.)_


**Show Image 4A-9: A legacy that lives on**

The ideas that William Penn brought to

Pennsylvania didn’t stay there. They spread

to other colonies. Eventually, Penn’s ideas

helped shape the whole country. Pennsylvania

became an example of what was possible

when people were given religious freedom and

were governed by laws based on the idea that

all people were created equal in the eyes of God.


Even today, we can see William Penn’s dream alive in our country. The idea

that we should all be free to believe what we want and live in peace is still

something we strive for. William Penn had to fight for religious freedom

during his lifetime. We have religious freedom today because he did.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (5 MIN.)**~~


**1. Inferential.** What were some of the ways William Penn worked towards
religious freedom? _(He spoke out publicly about religious freedom, and he_
_wrote a book while he was in jail called No Cross, No Crown. He also moved to_
_the colonies in Pennsylvania to create a colony founded on religious freedom_
_where anyone was welcome.)_


**2. Literal.** Which group of people made the colony of Pennsylvania their home?
_(Quakers)_


**3. Inferential** Why was Pennsylvania considered a very diverse place? _(People_
_from all backgrounds and beliefs felt welcomed and accepted under William_
_Penn’s new laws, so many people moved to Pennsylvania for that reason.)_


**4. Evaluative** How do you think William Penn would feel today knowing that one
of the principles the United States was built upon was freedom from religious
discrimination? _(Answers may vary but could include that he would feel happy_
_and satisfied that all of his work paid off and had a lasting influence.)_


~~**WORD WORK: TREATY (5 MIN.)**~~


1. In the Read-Aloud you heard, “William Penn met with the Lenape and offered
to make a treaty, or agreement, with them.”


2. Say the word _treaty_ with me.


3. The word treaty means a formal agreement.


4. “Both leaders signed the treaty so that their people could live in peace.”


5. Have you ever come to an important agreement on something? Can you
think of a situation where a written document would help make an agreement
stand out? [Ask two or three students.]


6. What’s the word we’ve been talking about?


**Discussion Activity.** Direct students to work with a partner. They should
explain to their partner what a treaty is and a time in the future they could
see a treaty being helpful.

~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**Age of Enlightenment, n.** a time in history when people made a lot of
advancements in science, art, and government

Example: There was much to learn about the world during the Age of
Enlightenment.
Variation(s): none


**stalls, n.** places where goods are sold in a market

Example: I looked around the many stalls at the market to find something
that I wanted.
Variation(s): stall


**haggle, v.** to discuss the price of something

Example: Sometimes, my grandmother will haggle with the grocer about the
price of vegetables.
Variation(s): haggles, haggled


**textiles, n.** types of fabric or cloth

Example: My clothes are made of different textiles.
Variation(s): textile


**pacifists, n.** people who do not believe in violence

Example: Because of their belief in nonviolence, the Quakers were pacifists.
Variation(s): pacifist
