~~**“PEACEFUL ABOLITION” (15 MIN.)**~~


**Show Image 5A-1: New ideas**

In 1776, the United States of America became

a country of its own. Before then, people living

in the colonies had been part of the British

Empire, under the rule of the British king.

The colonists wanted to be free to make their

own decisions. One of the Founding Fathers,

a man named Thomas Jefferson, wrote that “all men are created equal.”

~~_[Sometimes, the word men means all people, including both women and_~~

~~_men.]_~~ America’s founders thought people were equal in the eyes of God. The

Founding Fathers thought Americans should be free and should have the

chance to find happiness by pursuing their own dreams without government

interference.


But Jefferson’s words represented very new ideas in human history. In most

countries at the time, people did not have many freedoms. As we read before,

people in England did not have the religious freedom to worship unless they

worshiped exactly as the government required. And that was not the only

way people were not free. Throughout the British Empire at the time, in

England and its colonies, there were people who did not have even the basic

freedom to make choices of how to live or work—they were enslaved.


**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen actively, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses;
**TEKS 2.6.A** Establish purpose for reading assigned and self-selected texts; **TEKS 2.6.B** Generate questions about text
before, during, and after reading to deepen understanding and gain information; **TEKS 2.7.C** Use text evidence to support an
appropriate response; **TEKS 2.7.F** Respond using newly acquired vocabulary as appropriate.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Show Image 5A-2: Age of Enlightenment**

The Founding Fathers were influenced by many

great thinkers from around the world. New

ideas were popping up everywhere in these

times. Historians call this period the **Age of**

**Enlightenment** . ~~_[The Age of Enlightenment_~~

~~_refers to a time in history when people made a_~~

~~_lot of advancements in science, art, and government. It was said that theworld_~~

~~_was lighting up with new ideas and knowledge.]_~~ Some of those new ideas were

about how people should be treated. Enlightenment Age thinkers combined

old religious ideas with new ideas about law, concluding that if we truly were all

equal in the eyes of God, then the laws of a nation should treat people equally.

This seems normal to us today, but it was a very new idea 300 years ago.


**Show Image 5A-3: London, England**

One of the topics they began to consider

was slavery. Enslaved people were owned by

another person and did not have freedom

to make choices about how they worked or

lived. The idea of slavery is the opposite of

freedom. Today, we know slavery is wrong.

People should be free. But in the times of the American colonies, slavery

was considered by many to be normal. Slavery had existed in one form or

another for thousands of years. But the ideas of the Age of Enlightenment

began to have an effect, especially on America’s Founding Fathers and on

some of those in England that influenced them, like William Penn. ~~_What do_~~

~~_you remember about William Penn? What cause did he fight for?_~~ The founding

of America as a country began the process of ending slavery in the United

States. And around the same time as the Declaration of Independence, some

of the first big strides to end slavery throughout the British Empire also

began in the city of London in England. ~~_[Point to London on a map.]_~~


**Show Image 5A-4: Markets**

Imagine walking through the streets of

London in 1776. You would see busy markets

filled with colorful **stalls** . ~~_Stalls are where_~~

~~_people sell things in a market._~~ You would hear

people **haggling**, ~~_or discussing the prices of_~~

~~_things_~~, and the sounds of street musicians.



Support


To “enlighten” someone
means to explain
something to them.


Challenge


What do you think some
of the advancements in
science were during the
Age of Enlightenment?


Flip Book Poster 6M

might be more familiar
with the definition of _stall_
that means to pause or
wait. _Stall_ is a multiplemeaning word. In this text,
it means a place where
people sell things in a
market.



Lesson 5 Peaceful Abolition
**71**


Support


Industrialism means an
economy that has many
businesses and factories.

also grown in many areas

**72**



Everywhere you looked, there would be movement and energy. Business was

thriving because of a new idea that changed how people lived and worked. It

was called industrialism. During this time, new machines were invented that

made a whole lot of things easier to produce. Factories for these machines

started being built everywhere. One big industry in Great Britain was

**textiles**, ~~_or cloth and fabric_~~ .


A fourth of all the people in Great Britain were involved in working with

cotton in one way or another! Of all the cotton cloth made in England, the

British Empire exported half of it to other countries. The textile business

grew rapidly, and, thanks to industrial factories, prices kept getting lower so

more people could afford quality clothing. All of this business depended on

growing a lot of cotton. Back then, cotton was largely grown and picked by

enslaved people in places like North and South America.


**Show Image 5A-5: Sugar cane**

Besides textiles, or clothes, another major

industry in the British Empire back then

was sugar. Factories popped up throughout

England that would receive sugar cane, a type

of plant, and process it into various sugar

products like the kind of sugar we can buy

today. People loved to eat sugar, and the price of sugar steadily dropped

as industrial factories expanded. The source of the sugar eaten throughout

much of the world was sugar cane grown in many different parts of the

Americas, mostly in the Caribbean islands. And, back then, it was grown

mostly by people who were enslaved.


**Show Image 5A-6: Quakers**

The British Empire included many colonies

around the world where plantations, or large

farms, grew cotton or sugar cane with the

labor of enslaved people. Slavery was all too

common throughout the world. But as the

years went on, a few people in America and

England began to talk about doing away with slavery. They questioned how

one person could ever own another. It just didn’t seem right. They believed

that every person deserves freedom and respect. These people became

**abolitionist movement** . Among the earliest abolitionists were the Quakers.

The Quakers have a long history, in both Great Britain and the United States,


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


of standing up for the idea that people are equal in the eyes of God. Quakers

are **pacifists**, or ~~_people who do not believe in violence. Do you remember_~~

~~_the person we learned about in this unit who was a Quaker?_~~ They held to the

biblical teaching from Romans 12:17–18 that says, “Do not repay anyone evil

for evil. Be careful to do what is right in the eyes of everyone. If it is possible,

as far as it depends on you, live at peace with everyone.”


**Show Image 5A-7: Olaudah Equiano**

In the early years of the abolition movement,

a group of remarkable people emerged who

were determined to make a difference. One

of these was someone who had actually been

enslaved: Olaudah Equiano [/oo*lah*oo*dah

               - eh*kwaa*now/]. Born in Africa, he was

kidnapped as a child and sold into slavery in the British Empire. After many

tough years, he managed to buy his own freedom. He traveled to Great

Britain and, using his experiences, wrote _The Interesting Narrative of the Life_

_of Olaudah Equiano_ . This book described his life’s journey and the unfairness

of slavery. His book described his own Christian faith, and he asked how

people who followed the Golden Rule could possibly support slavery. As his

story spread, it opened many eyes to just how wrong slavery was.


**Show Image 5A-8: Phillis Wheatley**

Another former enslaved person had her own

story to tell. Once a young girl in West Africa,

Phillis Wheatley was taken from her home and

brought to Boston as an enslaved person. The

young Phillis discovered a talent for words.

She wrote beautiful poems. She was a woman

of strong Christian faith, and many of her writings spoke to the need of

God’s people to be free, including those who were then enslaved. Her poetry

achieved fame throughout America and England, and were an influence on

many of the Founding Fathers who advocated for the abolition of slavery.



Support


Review the quoted text
with students and explain
its meaning.“Do not
repay anyone evil for
evil.” _(If someone does_
_something bad to you,_
_don’t do something bad_
_back.)_ “Be careful to do
what is right in the eyes
of everyone.” _(You should_
_always do what is right.)_
“If it is possible, as far as
it depends on you, live
at peace with everyone.”
_(Live your life peacefully_
_and try not to engage in_
_conflict.)_


Support


Remind students of the
language of the Golden
Rule – “Do unto others as
you would have done unto
you” – and discuss what it
means.



Lesson 5 Peaceful Abolition
**73**


Support


The phrase _painting a_
_picture_ means to describe
something so that others
can begin to understand
and imagine it.


**74**



**Show Image 5A-9: Granville Sharp**

Granville Sharp was an English biblical scholar

who believed in using the law to protect those

in need. One day, he met a young enslaved

man in England named Jonathan Strong who

had been treated badly. Sharp decided to help,

and he argued in court that the man should be

free. A few years later, Sharp supported James Somerset, another enslaved

person in Britain. Somerset’s slaveholder wanted to sell him to a plantation

in Jamaica. Somerset had abolitionist friends like Granville Sharp, who

argued that this should not be allowed. This case was important because it

was the first time that a judge said that keeping someone enslaved should

not be allowed in England. The case did not end slavery in the British Empire,

but it was a major step forward.


Check for Understanding


How do you think abolitionists felt after the English judge ruled
that keeping someone enslaved should not be allowed in England?
_(Answers may vary but may include thrilled, excited, like their hard_
_work was paying off)_


**Show Image 5A-10: Thomas Clarkson**

Another early British abolitionist was a friend

of Sharp named Thomas Clarkson. Clarkson

was always on the move. He traveled all

over England, talking to sailors and others,

gathering stories about the unfairness of

slavery. Clarkson was determined to show

everyone how bad slavery was. With each story, he helped the world see why

slavery needed to end.


These individuals were not the only ones making a difference. A group

in England called the Society for the Purpose of Effecting the Abolition

of the Slave Trade was formed in 1787. Led by twelve dedicated Christian

abolitionists, including Sharp and Clarkson, the group gathered stories

about the slave trade. They turned these stories into flyers and articles that


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


reached people all over the country. Their mission was clear: gather support

to end slavery in Great Britain. Together, these abolitionists painted a picture

of a world where everyone had the right to be free.


**Show Image 5A-11: Taking a stand**

Not everyone believed what the abolitionists

did. Many abolitionists faced criticism and

even anger from others. Some shopkeepers

who supported abolition lost customers.

Artists who made paintings or drawings about

freedom faced criticism. Speakers who voiced

their ideas were sometimes met with loud boos instead of applause. And

some were attacked. ~~_Think about how hard it would be if you stood up for_~~

~~_something you believed in, and people got angry. How would that make you_~~

~~_feel?_~~


Despite all these challenges, abolitionists didn’t back down. They knew they

were standing up for what was right. These early abolitionists would light the

way to ending the slave trade. ~~_What does the phrase “light the way” mean?_~~

It was just the beginning of a longer journey. Their work reminds us all of an

important lesson. When you truly believe in something, even when it’s hard,

you should speak up for what is right, even if you might face consequences.

example, you were asked a few minutes ago, “Why did abolitionists want to
end slavery?” Turn to your neighbor and ask a different _why_ question. Listen
to your neighbor’s response. Then your neighbor will ask a new _why_ question,

Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Lesson 6: Wilberforce: English Abolitionist**
##### Read-Aloud



30M



**Reading:** Students will explain how William Wilberforce fought to end slavery in
the British Empire.


~~**TEKS 2.1.A; TEKS 2.6.A; TEKS 2.7.C**~~


**Language:** Students will demonstrate an understanding of the Tier 3 word _moral_ .


~~**TEKS 2.7.F**~~


~~**PURPOSE FOR LISTENING**~~


  - Tell students to listen carefully to find out how William Wilberforce fought to
end slavery in the British Empire.


~~**“WILBERFORCE: ENGLISH ABOLITIONIST” (15 MIN.)**~~


**Show Image 6A-1: William Wilberforce**

Over three hundred years ago, many things

were different from today. Things we take

for granted today, like running water, air

conditioning, or electric lights, just didn’t

exist yet. ~~_What does “take for granted” mean?_~~

People worked differently, played differently,

and lived differently. One thing that was legal

then that is not now was slavery. Slavery was when people were owned by

other people and did not have freedom to make choices about how they

worked or lived. But there were people who wanted to change how others

thought about slavery. One of them was an English man named William

Wilberforce. He believed that treating people as property was immoral, ~~_or_~~

~~_wrong_~~ ~~.~~ He also believed no one should be enslaved. He played a large role in

abolishing slavery in his country. This is his story.


**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen actively, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses;
**TEKS 2.6.A** Establish purpose for reading assigned and self-selected texts; **TEKS 2.7.C** Use text evidence to support an
appropriate response; **TEKS 2.7.F** Respond using newly acquired vocabulary as appropriate.



Support


Remind students that
they heard the phrase
“take for granted”
in Lesson 1. Briefly
explain the meaning
of the phrase “take for
granted” to support
students in making
connections.



Lesson 6 Wilberforce: English Abolitionist
**85**


Support


Tell students that
_encourage_ means
to support or show
confidence towards
someone or something.


Support


Tell students that in the
United States, this group
is called the ASPCA.
ASPCA stands for the
American Society for the
Prevention of Cruelty to
Animals.


**86**



**Show Image 6A-2: His early life**

Wilberforce was born in England in 1759. His

family was wealthy. They made their money

in the sea trade. This means they used ships

to buy and sell goods from distant places.

When Wilberforce was young, he went to live

with his aunt and uncle. His aunt and uncle

were devout Christians. This meant they

placed great importance on the Bible and its

teachings. It also meant their beliefs played

a large part in their daily decisions and actions. ~~_Who else have you learned_~~

~~_about whose faith was very important to them?_~~ Growing up with them,

Wilberforce began to believe this way too. His faith would guide many of the

big choices he made throughout his life.


**Show Image 6A-3: Education**

Wilberforce attended the University of

Cambridge. He soon met another student

named William Pitt, and they quickly became

close friends. They loved talking about how

to solve the problems they saw around them.

Both young men were determined to make

the world a better place. Pitt would become

the Prime Minister of England, a very important figure who reported directly

to the King of England. Pitt encouraged Wilberforce to become a Member of

Parliament. This meant Wilberforce would be one of many people who helped

run the government in England. He would have a say in what laws were made.


**Show Image 6A-4: A Moral Leader**

In Parliament, Wilberforce became famous for

his speeches. People listened closely when he

spoke because he was so enthusiastic about

helping others. His strong faith played a big

part in this. He believed in doing what was

**moral**, ~~_or what was right and fair_~~ . For example,

he thought it was important that workers get

a day to rest each week and pushed for a law where everyone would have

Sundays off. He also didn’t like it when animals were treated badly. This led

him to help start a group to protect animals called The Royal Society for the

Prevention of Cruelty to Animals (RSPCA), which still exists today.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Show Image 6A-5: Devoted to ending slavery**

Wilberforce made a friend named John

Newton. Newton used to be a captain of a

ship that carried enslaved people from Africa.

But eventually, he gave up his job as a ship

captain and became a devout Christian like

Wilberforce. Newton shared many stories

with Wilberforce about his time as a slave ship

captain. These stories shocked Wilberforce with the harsh realities of slavery.

These stories made Wilberforce even more sure that slavery was wrong.

Newton also wrote a famous song called “Amazing Grace,” which talked about

his own life and how he had changed. It described how he stopped living an

immoral life and began living a moral one. ~~_Have you ever heard that song?_~~


**Show Image 6A-6: Slavery in the British**
**Empire**

In the late 1700s, Wilberforce’s country of

England played a big part in the slave trade.

This meant they used ships to take people

from Africa to be enslaved in far-off places.

From the years 1700 to 1807, Britain enslaved

about 3.1 million Africans. Big cities in Britain

like Bristol, Liverpool, and Glasgow were the home ports for ships that

transported the enslaved people. The people who owned these ships made a

lot of money.


One day, Wilberforce realized just how strongly he felt about his beliefs. He

wondered if he should stop being a leader in Parliament. Slavery was still

legal in England, and he knew that many members of Parliament felt that it

should remain that way. But his friends told him that he could do the most

good by using his leadership skills to help others. He soon made up his mind.

He decided that he would use his position in Parliament to end slavery. He

said, “My business is in the world, and I must mix in the assemblies of men or

quit the post which Providence seems to have assigned me.” ~~_This was his way_~~

~~_of saying he needed to be where he could help make positive changes._~~



Support


Remind students that in
Lesson 5, they learned the
definition of _immoral_ . Tell
students that _immoral_ and
_moral_ are antonyms, or
words that have opposite
meanings. Have students
discuss the difference
between the two.


Challenge


Play a version of “Amazing
Grace” for students and
have them compare it to
the “Battle Hymn of the
Republic” that they heard
during the earlier unit on
the Civil War.


Support


Tell students that a port is
a place where ships dock
to load or unload items.



Lesson 6 Wilberforce: English Abolitionist
**87**


**88**



**Show Image 6A-7: Wilberforce’s long road to**
**abolition**

In 1791, Wilberforce tried to pass a law in

Parliament that would end the slave trade,

but it didn’t work. Why not? Many people in

England made a lot of money from the slave

trade. They didn’t want it to stop because

they would lose money. Those people were

not happy with Wilberforce for wanting to end the slave trade. They insulted

him and even threatened to hurt him. Some of the leaders who used to be his

friends didn’t talk to him anymore. But he didn’t give up. He knew he had to

keep trying. He knew that his opinions would make him unpopular. He would

never be as powerful or wealthy as others. Wilberforce knew these things, but

he chose to work on making life better for others.


Over time, more people in England began to agree with Wilberforce. They

listened to the stories of people who had once been enslaved. One man

named Olaudah Equiano [/ow*laa*duh eh*kwaa*now/], ~~_whom you learned_~~

~~_about in the previous lesson_~~, wrote a book about the terrible things he went

through as an enslaved person. These stories made many people very sad

and angry. The desire to abolish slavery spread all throughout England. ~~_What_~~

~~_does it mean to abolish something?_~~


**Show Image 6A-8: Wilberforce’s legacy**

In 1807, a law called the Slave Trade Act was

passed. This law said people in England could

not buy or sell enslaved people anymore, but it

didn’t say they could not own enslaved people.

Wilberforce knew there was more work to do.

He had spoken out against the bad treatment

of enslaved people for many, many years.

In 1833, after working very hard for a long time, Wilberforce helped pass a

new law. This law was the Slavery Abolition Act. ~~_What does abolition mean?_~~

At last, it was against the law to buy, sell, or own an enslaved person in the

British Empire. Because of this law, about 800,000 enslaved people were

set free. By 1838, slavery was completely gone from Britain. The powerful

British navy helped to enforce the law. The big naval ships stopped over

1,600 ships that were carrying enslaved people despite the law. Before long,

people in other countries saw what Wilberforce and his friends had done.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


They believed they could stop slavery in their countries too. In this way,

Wilberforce was inspiring the world!


The elimination of slavery in the British Empire was a major change in

human history. However, slavery was still practiced in most other nations

of the world at that time. Many abolitionists in the United States shared

Wilberforce’s faith and commitment to abolition. In the United States, the

Civil War was ultimately fought to end slavery. In the years that followed,

slavery began to be outlawed in more countries around the world. But it all

started with William Wilberforce. Because of him, many learned that one

person with strong faith can make a big difference.


~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


**1. Literal.** What group was William Wilberforce a member of that allowed him to
help run the government in England and have a say in what laws were made?
_(Parliament)_


**2. Literal.** In England, William helped pass what law in 1833 that said it was
against the law to buy, sell, or own an enslaved person in the British Empire?
_(The Slavery Abolition Act)_


**3. Inferential.** Was William a moral person? Why, or why not? What in the ReadAloud supports your thinking? _(Answers may vary but should include support_
_from the Read-Aloud: William thought workers deserved getting a day of rest,_
_protected animals who were treated badly, and fought to abolish slavery.)_

**Definition Detective: Moral**


 - In the Read-Aloud you heard, “He believed in doing what was moral, or what
was right and fair.”


 - Have students work with a partner to think of other meanings for moral.


Write the following definitions on the board or chart paper:


1. what is right and fair


2. a lesson or message that an author shares


 - Have students discuss with a partner which definition show how the word
_moral_ is used in the lesson _(one)_ .

_eliminate_ is a synonym for
_abolish_ . Knowing that, ask
students what _eliminate_
means.

Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Lesson 6: Wilberforce: English Abolitionist**
##### Application



20M



**Writing:** Students will write their own free verse poem in which they express an
opinion about William Wilberforce.


~~**TEKS 2.9.D.i; TEKS 2.12.A**~~


~~**TIMELINE (5 MIN.)**~~


  - Display the Fighting for a Cause Timeline started in Lesson 2.


  - Review the individuals placed on the timeline thus far.


  - Show students Image Card 5 (William Wilberforce), and have them describe
the important work he did as an abolitionist to end slavery.


  - Remind students that William Wilberforce was a member of Parliament
who helped make laws in England. A major law that he helped pass was the
Slavery Abolition Act in 1833. This law made slavery illegal.


  - Explain that William Wilberforce was alive and fought to abolish slavery
during the abolitionist movement. Have students determine the correct
location on the timeline for Image Card 6, and then place it to the right of
Image Card 5 to represent the passing of the Slavery Abolition Act of 1833.


~~**FREE VERSE WRITING (15 MIN.)**~~


  - Have students identify the type of poetry they wrote in the previous lessons.
_(free verse)_


  - Explain to students that they are going to write a free verse poem in which
they express their opinion about William Wilberforce. Provide some examples
of topics for poems such as: Wilberforce’s character, his achievements, or
the abolition movement in England.


  - Remind students that they have previously learned the four-step process
involved in writing free verse poetry. Have one or more students describe the
steps involved in this activity:


    - Students will work with a partner to brainstorm ideas about ideas about
individuals from the Read-Aloud.

##### Pausing Point


~~**NOTE TO TEACHER**~~


You should pause here and spend one day reviewing, reinforcing, or
extending the material taught thus far.


You may have students do any combination of the following activities, but
it is highly recommended that you use the Mid-Unit Assessment to assess
students’ knowledge of the individuals who fought for a cause. The other
activities may be done in any order. You may also choose to do an activity
with the whole class or with a small group of students who would benefit
from the particular activity.


~~**CORE CONTENT OBJECTIVES UP TO THIS PAUSING POINT**~~


Students will:


  - Describe how the abolitionist movement helped change laws and improve the
lives of many people


  - Explain why fighting for important causes has helped to change laws and
improve the lives of many people


  - Explain the term _discrimination_


  - Describe the lives and contributions of Queen Esther, William Penn, and
William Wilberforce


  - Identify the main causes for which Queen Esther, William Penn, and William
Wilberforce fought during their lifetimes

~~**ACTIVITIES**~~


**Key Vocabulary Brainstorming**


Materials: Chart paper, chalkboard, or whiteboard

 - Give students a key unit concept or vocabulary word such as _religious_
_freedom._


 - Have them brainstorm everything that comes to mind when they hear the
word, such as discrimination, civil rights, Esther, Jews, William Penn, Quakers,
pacifist, nonviolence, etc.


 - Record their responses on the board/chart paper for reference.


**Image Review**

retell the Read-Aloud using the images.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause



**94**


**Riddles for Core Content**

  - Ask students riddles such as the following to review core content:


    - I became a member of Parliament, and I worked tirelessly to pass a law
that would end slavery in the British Empire. _(William Wilberforce)_


    - I was Queen of Persia. I risked my life to save the Jewish people living in
Persia. _(Queen Esther)_


    - I was a Quaker. Religious freedom was my fight, and I founded the colony
of Pennsylvania on the principle of religious freedom for all. _(William Penn)_


**Class Book: Fighting for a Cause**


Materials: Drawing paper, drawing tools

  - Tell the class or a group of students that they are going to make a class book
to help them remember what they have learned thus far in this unit.


  - Have students brainstorm important information about fighting for a cause
and about the people they learned about who fought for the rights of others
(e.g., Queen Esther, William Penn, and William Wilberforce).


  - Have each student choose one idea to draw a picture of, and ask them to
write a caption for the picture.


  - Bind the pages to make a book to put in the class library for students to read
again and again. You may choose to add more pages upon completion of the
entire unit before binding the book.


Pausing Point
**95**


**96**



**Writing Prompts**

 - Students may be given an additional writing prompt such as the following:


   - If I became a member of my country’s government, like William Wilberforce,
I would promote . . .


   - People choose to fight for a cause because . . .


   - Religious freedom and freedom from slavery are both important because. . .


**Image Card Review**

 - Help students identify all of the individuals on the Image Cards used for the
Fighting for a Cause Timeline, and have them share what has been learned
about each person.


 - Then pass out all of the Image Cards to various students.


 - Have students share with a partner at least one cause the individual fought
for and a fact from their life. For example, for the Image Card of William
Penn, a student might say, “William Penn believed that everyone should be
free to practice their religion how they wanted without interference from the
government.”


**Perspectives**

 - Remind students that all of the stories of all of the extraordinary individuals
they have learned about are set many years ago.


 - Tell them that they are going to write about their classroom through the eyes
of one of these individuals, pretending to describe their classroom from that
individual’s perspective, or point of view. Explain that perspective is how
someone sees or experiences something.


 - Elaborate, saying that if Queen Esther were to describe their classroom, she
would probably comment about how different the world is today than in her
time. Or, if William Wilberforce were to describe their classroom, he would
probably comment on the fact that all students and their parents are free,
that slavery was no longer permitted. If William Penn were to describe their
classroom, he would probably comment on the fact that there is a map of the
United States that shows Pennsylvania and that there might be a picture of the
Constitution in the classroom that shows the Bill of Rights. He would be glad to
know that freedom of religion is listed there.


 - Give students time to write their classroom description from the perspective
of one of these people.


 - Then, allow students to share their writing with the class.


 - Have students ask each other questions pertaining to the figure they have


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


chosen. Remember to expand upon each student’s writing and response(s)
using richer and more complex language, including, if possible, any unitrelated vocabulary.


**You Were There: Fighting for a Cause**

  - Have students pretend that they were with one of the individuals in this unit
as he or she fought for a cause.


  - Ask students to describe what they saw and heard. For example, for Queen
Esther, students may talk about how she had to hide her beliefs from the king
at first, but then she showed enormous bravery when she saved the Jewish
people living in Persia. Or they may pretend they were William Penn as he
fought for religious freedom in London, or they watched William Wilberforce
give a speech before Parliament.


  - Consider also extending this activity by adding group or independent writing
opportunities associated with the “You Were There” concept.


**Who Am I?**


Materials: Sticky notes

  - On a sticky note, write the name of each figure students have learned about
thus far in _Fighting for a Cause_ .


  - Make one sticky note for each student, so that there may be more than one
or two for each figure.


  - Don’t let students see the sticky notes before beginning the activity. Place a
name onto each student’s back. Don’t let them see the name that you put on
their back.


**• Directions:** You will each have the opportunity to ask five yes or no questions
to find out who you are. You might ask, “Am I female?” or “Did I fight for
religious freedom?” “Am I from England?” Do not ask one person all five
questions. You should move around the room and ask a different student
each of your yes or no questions.


  - After everyone has asked five questions, you will all try to guess who you are.
(Encourage students to use unit vocabulary when they ask their questions.)


  - You may add variety to the game by including objects or secondary
characters in the Read-Alouds, such as King Xerxes, King Charles II, the
Lenape Native Americans, or John Newton.

~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**boycott, v.** to stop using or buying the services or goods from a certain
country or company

Example: They boycotted the city’s bus system.
Variation(s): boycotts, boycotted


**Civil Rights Act, n.** a law passed in 1964 that made discrimination based on
race, color, religion, or nationality illegal

Example: In 1964, the U.S. Congress passed the Civil Rights Act.
Variation(s): none


**minister, n.** a leader in a church

Example: He is the minister of his church.
Variation(s): none


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**segregated, adj.** divided or separate from

Example: Before the Civil Rights Act was passed, places like restaurants and
movie theaters were segregated.
Variation(s): none


**advocating, v.** to strongly support

Example: The group was advocating for longer recess times.
Variation(s): advocate, advocates, advocated


**backlash, n.** a strong reaction to something from a large group of people

Example: The group received backlash from the public after their last stunt.
Variation(s): none

_Wilberforce, the abolitionist movement)_

abolitionist himself. What were abolitionists fighting for? _(to abolish slavery)_


  - Remind students that the word _fight_ here does not mean to physically strike
someone. Have students identify the word used to describe the method
of fighting for rights in which someone does not physically strike back.
_(nonviolence)_ Have students identify the word for people who do not believe
in using violence. _(pacifist)_

person. Remind students that a movement is when a group of people work
together to make a change.

Cesar Chavez, Dolores Huerta, and Dr. Héctor P. García. Ask students if they
have ever heard of any of these people’s names.

Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


  - Explain that today students will hear about multiple people who helped
improve the lives of others in the United States. Explain to students that none
of the people they will hear about today were presidents, and most did not
serve any role in the government; they were regular people who saw some
unfairness, and they tried to change what they saw.

~~**“WORKING FOR CIVIL RIGHTS” (15 MIN.)**~~


**Show Image 7A-1: Working for Civil Rights**

Throughout history, people have fought for

religious freedom, ~~_the ability to worship and_~~

~~_practice the religion of their choosing without_~~

~~_government interference_~~ . They worked to

convince others of the need for religious

freedom, even at great personal risk. ~~_What_~~

~~_does “great personal risk” mean?_~~ People have

also fought to end slavery. Slavery is wrong, but it was practiced in most

nations throughout history. Over time, many people, driven by the conviction,

or belief, that we are all equal in the eyes of God, recognized that slavery was

wrong and worked to convince others that it must end.


In our history, much success was achieved by people **advocating** for these

changes without resorting to violence. They would give speeches, write

books or poetry, work as lawyers, or share business opportunities that

helped provide freedom. Ultimately, the U.S. Civil War ended the practice of

slavery in our country. The ideas of the Founding Fathers and the ideas that

emerged in the Age of Enlightenment continued to impact the country well

after the Civil War. In a country built on the idea that “All Men are Created

Equal,” the laws of the country needed to provide equal protection for all of

its citizens. But for many years after the Civil War, that was still not the case.


**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen actively, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses;
**TEKS 2.6.A** Establish purpose for reading assigned and self-selected texts; **TEKS 2.6.E** Make connections to personal
experiences, ideas in other texts, and society; **TEKS 2.6.F** Make inferences and use evidence to support understanding;
**TEKS 2.7.F** Respond using newly acquired vocabulary as appropriate.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Show Image 7A-2: The Civil Rights**
**Movement**

In the years leading up to the 1950s and

1960s, many Black Americans were subject

to discrimination based on the color of their

skin. Often, they lived in states where people

of different races were legally **segregated** .

~~_Segregation means that people of different_~~

~~_races were kept apart by law in schools, restaurants, buses, and many other_~~

~~_places._~~ They couldn’t use the same bathrooms or even drink from the same

water fountains. These rules and laws that required segregation were known

as the Jim Crow laws. They were enforced in the southern parts of the United

States, making life difficult for many Black Americans.


People all over the United States did not just accept these unfair rules. More

and more, they started to speak up to demand change. In the following

decades, a movement began to grow, which was called the civil rights

movement. This movement, which drew more support with each passing

year, worked to make sure everyone, no matter their skin color, had the same

rights under the law. Before positive changes could happen, many brave

people had to step forward. They were willing to speak out for what was right,

even if it meant facing anger, threats, or even getting hurt. These people were

willing to face **backlash** from others because they wanted to stand up for

what was right. ~~_Backlash is a strong reaction to something from a large group_~~

~~_of people._~~


**Show Image 7A-3: Jackie Robinson**

One of the people who stood up for civil

rights was Jackie Robinson. Jackie Robinson

was well-educated and a terrific athlete. He

became the first African American to play

Major League Baseball. When Jackie first

began playing Major League Baseball, some of

his fellow team members and fans treated him

badly. Jackie Robinson did not give up. Because of his courage and talent, he

changed people’s attitudes, ~~_or their views about things. What is courage?_~~



Support


What is another
movement you have
learned about? What
cause did it support?



Lesson 7 Working for Civil Rights
**105**


Support


Write the statement from
Dr. King Jr. on the board,
and review its meaning
with students.


**106**



**Show Image 7A-4: Rosa Parks**

Another fighter for civil rights was Rosa Parks.

On a cold December evening many years ago,

Parks, an African American woman, sat on a

crowded bus in Alabama. When the bus driver

asked her to give up her seat to a white man

who did not have a seat, she refused. She

found the courage to say, “No.” After Parks

was arrested, people came together and decided that they wouldn’t ride the

Montgomery City buses until everyone had the same rights as white people.

~~_How does this image help you understand what this text says about Rosa_~~

~~_Parks?_~~


**Show Image 7A-5: Martin Luther King Jr.**

Around the same time, a man named Dr.

Martin Luther King Jr. also wanted to make

things fair for everyone. He was a leader who

had big dreams of a better world. He was a

**minister**, ~~_which means he was a leader in_~~

~~_his church_~~ . His beliefs in God, peace, and

fairness were very important to him. In fact,

he supported the bus **boycott** that Rosa Parks began. ~~_Boycott means to stop_~~

~~_using or buying services or goods from a certain company._~~ He helped African

Americans come together and support each other during those long days

when they chose not to ride the bus.


Martin Luther King Jr. had an important idea: Change should come from

talking, understanding, and acting peacefully. He did not believe in fighting

or hurting others. Dr. King Jr. said, “Darkness cannot drive out darkness; only

light can do that. Hate cannot drive out hate; only love can do that.” ~~_What do_~~

~~_you think that statement means?_~~


**Show Image 7A-6: Cesar Chavez and Dolores**
**Huerta**

While many were speaking out for rights in

cities, there were also people working for

fairness in the fields and on farms throughout

the American Southwest. Cesar Chavez and

Dolores Huerta were two brave leaders who

wanted better lives for farm workers. During


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


the 1960s, many workers in the fields were being treated very unfairly. They

worked long hours under the hot sun, and they didn’t get paid very much.

Many of these workers were Hispanic, and they faced a lot of discrimination.

In 1962, Chavez and Huerta organized thousands of farmworkers into a

group together. Because so many people came together and demanded

change, the two found a way to improve the lives of all farmworkers, which

was part of the civil rights movement to improve equal protection under the

law for all Americans.


**Show Image 7A-7: Dr. Héctor P. García**

Cesar Chavez was a veteran who fought for

our country in World War II. After the war was

over, he kept fighting, but this time without

violence, and instead with his words. Another

veteran of World War II who did the same was

Dr. Héctor P. García. During the war, he won

a medal for his bravery. After the war was

over, he worked as a doctor to take care of people. Even more, he was one

of many people around our country who began demanding civil rights for

all Americans, to make sure that all American citizens were treated fairly.

He worked with many political leaders to make sure the laws of our country

provided equal protection to Hispanic Americans.


**Show Image 7A-8: Civil Rights Act**

All of the pressure from the civil rights

movement resulted in the signing of the

Civil Rights Act of 1964. ~~_The Civil Rights Act_~~

~~_prevented anyone from being discriminated_~~

~~_against because of their race, color, religion,_~~

~~_sex, or national origin._~~ Soon after the Civil

Rights Act became law, another important

law was passed. The Voting Rights Act of 1965 made sure that everyone,

no matter their race, could vote in elections. Before this law, many Black

Americans in the South were prevented from voting. With these new laws,

the United States began to change. Schools, parks, restaurants, and offices

started to welcome everyone, no matter their race.

~~**ELPS 3.G; ELPS 4.J**~~


**108**



Even when things were difficult, brave people from all over the country never

gave up. They faced mean words, angry shouts, and even danger. They held

onto their dreams of a place where everyone had freedom and was treated

equally under the law. When people come together with courage to fight for

freedom under the law, they can create amazing changes that last a long time.


~~**DISCUSSING THE READ-ALOUD (10 MIN.)**~~


**1. Inferential.** What are some of the civil rights guaranteed by the United
States? _(Answers may vary but may include freedom of speech, the right to_
_vote, protection from discrimination, and equal protection under the law.)_


**2. Literal.** What actions did Jackie Robinson, Rosa Parks, Martin Luther King
Jr., Cesar Chavez, and Dolores Huerta take during the civil rights movement
to show that certain people were being discriminated against? _(Answers_
_may vary but should include support from the Read-Aloud: Robinson was the_
_first African American to play Major League Baseball; Parks refused to give_
_up her bus seat because of her skin color; MLK Jr. supported the bus boycott_
_and helped Black Americans support each other; Chavez and Huerta helped_
_organize Hispanic farmworkers to be free from discrimination.)_


**3. Literal.** Which law was passed in the United States that prevented people
from being discriminated against because of their race, color, religion, sex, or
national origin? _(The Civil Rights Act of 1964)_


**4. Evaluative.** Think-Pair-Share: What was the civil rights movement? _(A series_
_of events in the United States from the 1950s to the 1960s that involved a_
_large number of people fighting to ensure all people were treated equally_
_under the law.)_


~~**WORD WORK: ADVOCATING (5 MIN.)**~~


1. Remind students that in the Read-Aloud they heard, “In our history, much
success was achieved by people advocating for these changes without
resorting to violence.”


2. Ask students to say the word _advocating_ with you.


3. Explain that when someone is advocating for something, it means to strongly
support it.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


4. Remind students that they’ve heard about abolitionists advocating to end
slavery and about others advocating for religious freedom.


5. Ask students to think of other examples of things people advocate for. When
have they advocated for something or someone? Encourage them to try to
use the word advocating when they talk about it. _[Ask two or three students._
_If necessary, guide and/or rephrase the students’ responses: “. . .”]_


6. What’s the word we’ve been talking about?

sure everyone, no matter their skin color, had the same rights under the law.

6, and then place it to the right of Image Card 5 to represent the passing of
the Civil Rights Act of 1964.

movement and the civil rights movement.


  - Tell students that the enlarged version of Activity Page 7.1 has the number “1”
written in the outer circle labeled “The Abolitionist Movement,” the number
“2” written in the center where circles overlap, and the number “3” written in
the outer circle labeled “The Civil Rights Movement.”


  - Have students complete the activity page in pairs. Then, have students share
their Venn diagrams with the class.

**Introducing the Read-Aloud**

 - Prepare and display a Somebody Wanted But So Then chart, similar to the
one below. Alternatively, you may access a digital copy in the online materials
for this unit.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**challenge, v.** to question whether something is right or wrong

Example: Knowing it was not up for discussion, my sister did not want to
challenge my parents’ decision to not get a pet.
Variation(s): challenges, challenged, challenging


**gamble, v.** to take a chance

Example: If you plan an outdoor picnic on a cloudy day, you gamble that it
will not rain.
Variation(s): gambles, gambled, gambling


**hostility, n.** a feeling of unfriendliness, anger, or dislike

Example: Instead of shaking hands at the end of the game, the other team
showed hostility by walking off without congratulating us on our win.
Variation(s): hostilities


**intimidate, v.** to try to make someone do something by making them afraid

Example: My older brother tried to intimidate me into doing his chores by
saying he would take away my toys if I didn’t cooperate.
Variation(s): intimidates, intimidated, intimidating


**teammates, n.** members of the same side working together for a
common goal

Example: The teammates worked together to pass the ball down the court
and win the game.
Variation(s): teammate

Lesson 8 Jackie Robinson: Champion of Equality

**118**



**Lesson 8: Jackie Robinson: Champion of Equality**
30M
##### Read-Aloud


**Reading:** Students will explain how Jackie Robinson fought for civil rights
in sports.


~~**TEKS 2.1.A; TEKS 2.6.A; TEKS 2.7.C**~~


**Language:** Students will demonstrate an understanding of the Tier 2
word _challenge_ .


~~**TEKS 2.7.F**~~


~~**PURPOSE FOR LISTENING**~~


  - Tell students to listen carefully to find out how Jackie Robinson fought for
civil rights in sports.


~~**“JACKIE ROBINSON: CHAMPION OF EQUALITY” (15 MIN.)**~~


**Show Image 8A-1: Jackie Robinson**

Baseball has been a popular sport for a very

long time. But did you know that there was a

time when African American baseball players

and white baseball players were not allowed to

play together? This all changed when a man

named Jackie Robinson showed the world

what a great player he was. He showed the

world how much courage he had, too. ~~_What does the word_~~ ~~courage~~ ~~_mean?_~~

~~_(bravery when facing a dangerous or difficult situation)_~~ This is the incredible

story of Jackie Robinson.


**Show Image 8A-2: Jackie was the youngest**
**of five children**

Jack Roosevelt (Jackie) Robinson was

born in 1919, in Cairo, Georgia. He was the

youngest of five children. His parents were

sharecroppers—people who worked the land

for others for very little pay.


**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen actively, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses;
**TEKS 2.6.A** Establish purpose for reading assigned and self-selected texts; **TEKS 2.7.C** Use text evidence to support an
appropriate response; **TEKS 2.7.F** Respond using newly acquired vocabulary as appropriate.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


Jackie’s father left his family when Jackie was a baby. His mother, Mallie

Robinson, moved the family to Pasadena, California, to be near relatives

who would help her raise her children. The Robinsons lived in a small house

on Pepper Street. Mallie worked many different jobs to support her family.

Despite hardships, Jackie grew up in a close and loving family.


**Show Image 8A-3: Jackie played many sports**

From an early age, Jackie was a talented

athlete. When he became a student at John

Muir High School, his brothers Mack and Frank

encouraged him to play sports. Jackie played

football, basketball, baseball, and tennis, and

he ran track, too. He played shortstop and

catcher on the baseball team, quarterback on

the football team, and guard on the basketball team. In track and field, he

won awards for the long jump.


After high school, Jackie attended Pasadena Junior College. Again, he played

basketball, football, and baseball, and he ran track. On the football team, he

played quarterback and safety. He was shortstop and leadoff hitter for the

baseball team, and he broke school long jump records.


**Show Image 8A-4: Jackie at UCLA**

Before long, the University of California in Los

Angeles, also known as UCLA, offered Jackie

an athletic scholarship. ~~_What is a scholarship?_~~

Jackie was happy to accept. While at UCLA,

Jackie proved himself to be a good student

and an amazing athlete. As a UCLA student, he

competed in four sports: baseball, basketball,

football, and track. Jackie was selected for the All-American football team,

which is a team of players from different schools who are the best players in

the country.


When Jackie left college, he began playing football for the Honolulu Bears, a

Hawaiian semi-professional team. When the United States became involved

in World War II, Jackie joined the United States Army. After two years, he was

promoted to the officer rank of second lieutenant.



Support


Review the meaning of
the word _hardship_ .


Support


Review what it means
to break records.



Lesson 8 Jackie Robinson: Champion of Equality
**119**


**120**



**Show Image 8A-5: Jackie in the military**

Growing up, Jackie had been aware of

discrimination. When Jackie joined the army,

he realized that there was discrimination there,

too. Jackie felt the need to **challenge** these

attitudes. ~~_When you challenge something, you_~~

~~_question whether something is right._~~


When Jackie was in the army, he refused an order to sit at the back of a

military bus. Jackie felt that the color of his skin should not determine where

he could or could not sit. Jackie was court-martialed, ~~_or charged with a crime,_~~

for refusing this order. However, at his trial, he was found to be not guilty.


**Show Image 8A-6: Jackie playing for the**
**Monarchs**

Jackie left the army toward the end of World

War II. He was signed to play shortstop for the

Kansas City Monarchs. The Monarchs were a

team that belonged to something called the

National Negro League. It was in this league

that African American players were allowed to

play baseball. Jackie traveled all over the Midwest during that season with

this league.


One day, a man named Branch Rickey saw Jackie play. Branch Rickey was

the president of the Brooklyn Dodgers. Branch thought that Jackie was an

incredible player. He wanted Jackie to play for his team. The only problem

was that the Brooklyn Dodgers was an all-white team. ~~_Back then, African_~~

~~_Americans and white people did not play professional sports together._~~


Branch met with Jackie and told him that he needed Jackie to be very brave.

He wanted Jackie to play in the all-white Major League Baseball program.

This was referred to as “breaking the color barrier.” This meant that, before

this time, a person’s skin color was a barrier to, or something that stopped

them from, playing in many sports leagues. Branch wanted Jackie to break

this barrier. He explained to Jackie that at first he would probably be treated

badly by most of the fans, and even by some of his fellow players. But Branch

was willing to **gamble**, ~~_or take a chance_~~ ~~,~~ that Jackie was strong enough to

break the color barrier and change attitudes. He believed that Jackie had

self-control and courage. ~~_What sort of person did Branch Rickey think Jackie_~~

~~_Robinson was?_~~


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


Before Jackie agreed, he asked Branch a question. Jackie asked, “Are you

looking for [someone] who is afraid to fight back?”


Branch replied, “No, I need a player with guts enough not to fight back.” ~~_What_~~

~~_is the word you learned that means responding to unfairness by refusing to_~~

~~_take physical action? (nonviolence)_~~ Jackie Robinson agreed to be that man.


**Show Image 8A-7: Jackie and Pee Wee Reese**

Jackie traveled to Daytona Beach, Florida,

for spring training. He began playing with

the Montreal Royals, a training team for the

Brooklyn Dodgers. Jackie played well. The

most difficult days for Jackie were often when

he was off the field and traveling with the

other players. During this time, because he

was African American, he was not allowed to stay in the same hotels as his

**teammates** . ~~_Teammates are members of the same side._~~ He could not eat at

the same restaurants and diners. Jackie did not like this one bit, but he was

determined to become the first African American Major League Baseball

player in the United States.


And then it happened. Just six days before the start of the 1947 baseball

season, Jackie got the call that he had been waiting for. The Dodgers wanted

him to play. Sadly, not all of his teammates were happy about this. Some said

they would rather sit out and miss a game than play with Jackie. But Jackie’s

teammate, Pee Wee Reese, came to his defense. “You can hate a man for

many reasons,” Pee Wee said. “Color is not one of them.”


**Show Image 8A-8: Jackie playing for the**
**Brooklyn Dodgers**

On April 15, 1947, when Jackie put on the

Brooklyn Dodgers uniform, wearing number

forty-two, he broke the color barrier. As the

first African American player on a major league

team, Jackie made his debut at Ebbets Field

before a crowd of 26,623 people, including

more than 14,000 African American fans. ~~_How do you think Jackie felt on the_~~

~~_day he finally played for the Brooklyn Dodgers?_~~



Challenge


Have students discuss
why it might take more
courage to react in a
nonviolent way than
to react with physical
violence.


Support


Explain that the phrase
“made his debut” means
he played his first game.



Lesson 8 Jackie Robinson: Champion of Equality
**121**


Support


Explain that a single
in baseball is when
a player makes it
safely to first base
after hitting the ball;
a double is when the
player makes it safely
to second base; a triple
is when the player
makes it safely to third
base; and a home run is
when the player makes
it safely back to home
plate after hitting the
ball.


**122**



Jackie knew that he would have to be strong and concentrate on the game

and nothing else. At first, there was a great deal of **hostility** . ~~_Hostility is a_~~

~~_feeling or an act of unfriendliness, anger, or dislike._~~ People called him names,

but Jackie just played baseball. Some of his teammates would not sit with

him. No matter! Game after game, Jackie focused on playing, even when

pitchers threw balls and tried to hit him.


**Show Image 8A-9: Jackie at bat**

In one game during his first year as a

professional player, Jackie received a seven
inch gash, or big cut, on his leg. It was a

deliberate injury inflicted on him during

a game between the Dodgers and the

Philadelphia Phillies. It was an attempt to

**intimidate** him. ~~_The word_~~ ~~intimidate~~ ~~_means_~~

~~_to try to make someone do something by making them afraid._~~ Still, Jackie

refused to quit. He simply said, “I’m not concerned with your liking or

disliking me . . . all I ask is that you respect me as a human being.” ~~_Do you_~~

~~_think Jackie Robinson had courage?_~~


Later in his baseball career, Jackie hit a home run, a triple, a double, and a

single in the same game.


Jackie could run, hit, steal bases, and play second base like nobody else. His

friend and teammate Duke Snider said, “He was the greatest competitor I

have ever seen.”


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Show Image 8A-10: Jackie as Rookie of the**
**Year**

By the end of his first year in the major

leagues, Jackie had played in 151 games for

the Dodgers, scored 125 runs, and had 175

hits, including thirty-one doubles, five triples,

and twelve home runs. He was named Rookie

of the Year. And in 1949 he was chosen as the most valuable player in the

National League. Jackie had proven that the world of professional sports is

far better when everyone can participate in it.


During Jackie’s career, the Dodgers played in six World Series. Jackie played

in every one. He could hit, and he was fast. He averaged more than 110 runs

per season from 1947 to 1953 and had a .311 career batting average. Jackie

helped the Dodgers win six National League pennants and one World Series

title. These impressive achievements make Jackie Robinson one of the best

players to have ever played Major League Baseball.


Jackie retired from baseball in 1957. Jackie said, “The way I figured it, I was

even with baseball and baseball was even with me. The game had done much

for me, and I had done much for it.”


**Show Image 8A-11: Jackie’s number is**
**honored**

Jackie Robinson was the first African

American inducted into the Baseball Hall of

Fame. He received many other honors, too. In

1997, Major League Baseball honored Jackie

by retiring his number from all Major League

Baseball teams. This is a very special honor, and it means that no other

major league player on any team can ever wear his number. ~~_What was the_~~

~~_number on Jackie Robinson’s shirt?_~~ Every year since 2004, however, all major

league players wear Jackie’s number, forty-two, every April 15 to remember

the important role Jackie Robinson played in Major League Baseball.



Support


Review the meaning of
the word _achievement_
from Lesson 1.
_(something gained_
_through hard work)_

~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


Check for Understanding


**Compare/Contrast:** In what ways was Jackie Robinson similar to
the people you heard about in the previous lesson? _(Rosa Parks,_
_Martin Luther King Jr., Cesar Chavez, Dolores Huerta, Héctor P._
_García)_ In what ways was Jackie’s fight for civil rights different?
_(Rather than march, campaign, or protest, he played a professional_
_sport and suffered through humiliation and hostility without letting_
_it stop him form becoming a great baseball player.)_


**1. Literal.** What happened to Jackie Robinson when he joined the major
leagues? _(Some players would not sit with him; he was called names; some_
_players wanted to physically hurt him; he was still not allowed to stay in the_
_hotels with his teammates.)_


**2. Inferential.** What kind of person was Branch Rickey looking for to break the
race barrier in professional baseball? _(Branch Rickey wanted someone with_
_self-control and courage—someone who could not be easily intimidated, who_
_would respond to unfair treatment with nonviolence.)_

   - **Literal.** What did Branch Rickey want Jackie to become? _(Branch Rickey_
_wanted Jackie to become the first African American to play in the all-white_
_Major League Baseball program.)_


**3. Literal.** What did Pee Wee Reese, Jackie’s teammate, do to help support
Jackie Robinson? _(Pee Wee Reese spoke up for Jackie and against racial_
_discrimination.)_


**4. Literal.** Jackie Robinson’s fight for fairness did not begin with playing
professional baseball, but, instead, it began when he was in the army. What
did Jackie do while he was in the army to challenge attitudes about race?
_(While in the army, Jackie refused to sit in the back of a military bus simply_
_because he was African American.)_


**5. Evaluative.** Think-Pair-Share: Why do you think Branch Rickey wanted a
player who would react nonviolently to the hostility and humiliation he knew
the player would face? _(Answers may vary.)_


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


~~**WORD WORK: CHALLENGE (5 MIN.)**~~


1. In the Read-Aloud you heard, “Jackie felt the need to challenge
these attitudes.”


2. Say the word _challenge_ with me.


3. When you _challenge_ something, you question whether that thing is right
or wrong.


4. The coach decided to challenge the umpire’s call that the player swung at
the pitch.


5. Can you think of times when you wanted to challenge someone or
something? Can you think of something you want to challenge? Try to use
the word _challenge_ when you tell about it. [Ask two or three students. If
necessary, guide and/or rephrase the students’ responses: “I wanted/want to
challenge . . .”]


6. What’s the word we’ve been talking about?


**Use a Horizontal Word Wall activity for follow-up.** We will make a
Horizontal Word Wall for the word _challenge_ .


 - Tell me what you think of when you hear the word _challenge_ . _(Answers may_
_vary but may include words such as_ question _and_ doubt _.)_ [For each word
proposed by students, as well as the words _challenge_, _question_, and _doubt_,
create a separate index card. Place the word _challenge_ and its synonyms on
one end of a horizontal line.]


 - Tell me what you think is the opposite of _challenge_ . _(Answers may vary_
_but may include words such as_ accept _,_ believe _, and_ trust _.)_ [For each word
proposed by students, as well as the words _accept_, _believe_, and _trust_, create a
separate index card. Place the words on the other end of the horizontal line.]


 - [After placing the initial set of words on the Horizontal Word Wall, have
students discuss the two groupings of words and consider how each word
differs from the others in its group. For example, students may say that
a person who doubts that something is true may not verbally challenge
what is being said. As a result, doubting something may be less strong than
challenging something.]



Support


If students have
difficulty generating
words on their own,
consider modeling the
use of a thesaurus.

Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**assign, v.** to give or to set apart something

Example: The airline was not able to assign our seats until the day of
the flight.
Variation(s): assigns, assigned, assigning


**boycott, n.** an action in which a group of people join together and refuse to
do business with a company or organization as a way of expressing strong
disapproval

Example: The community planned to stage a boycott of the store that
refused to recycle.
Variation(s): boycotts


**disrupted, v.** prevented something from happening by causing confusion

Example: The bad weather disrupted our plans.
Variation(s): disrupt, disrupts, disrupting


**injustice, n.** a lack of fairness

Example: The punishment John received was considered to be a terrible
injustice, as most people believed he was innocent of the crime.
Variation(s): injustices


**segregation, n.** the practice under law of keeping groups of people separate,
or apart, because of things such as race

Example: Because of segregation, Jackie Robinson was not able to eat at
the same restaurants as his teammates when they traveled to play baseball.
Variation(s): none















|Vocabulary Chart for “Rosa Parks: The Mother of the Civil Rights Movement”|Col2|Col3|Col4|
|---|---|---|---|
|**Type**|**Tier 3** <br>**Unit-Specifc Words**|**Tier 2** <br>**General Academic Words**|**Tier 1** <br>**Everyday Speech Words**|
|Vocabulary|boycott<br>segregation_(segregación)_|assign<br>disrupted<br>injustice_(injusticia)_||
|Multiple<br>Meaning||||
|Sayings<br>and Phrases|pushing us around<br>the strength of my ancestors with me<br>changed the course of history|pushing us around<br>the strength of my ancestors with me<br>changed the course of history|pushing us around<br>the strength of my ancestors with me<br>changed the course of history|

Pennsylvania. _(William Penn)_

to change attitudes when I was in the army and refused to sit at
the back of the bus only because I was African American. _(Jackie_
_Robinson)_


       - I fought to end slavery in the British Empire. _(William Wilberforce)_

Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


~~**ESSENTIAL BACKGROUND INFORMATION OR TERMS (5 MIN.)**~~


 - Explain that even though civil rights are guaranteed by law, many people
have been excluded from exercising their civil rights throughout history.


 - Tell students that the civil rights movement was a period of time in our
country’s history, from the 1950s to the 1960s, when people from many
races and different groups worked alongside African Americans in their fight
for civil rights, which later led to increased rights for people in many other
groups.


~~**IMAGE PREVIEW**~~


 - Explain that today students will be hearing about a woman named
Rosa Parks.


**Show Image 9A-5: Rosa sitting on the bus**

 - Tell students that events that occurred one evening while Rosa Parks was
riding a bus helped spark the civil rights movement.

the method of fighting
for rights in which
someone does not

_(nonviolence)_

**“ROSA PARKS: THE MOTHER OF THE CIVIL RIGHTS MOVEMENT”**
~~**(15 MIN.)**~~


**Show Image 9A-1: Rosa Parks**

Rosa Louise Parks was born a long time

ago, in 1913, in Tuskegee, Alabama. ~~_[Point to_~~

~~_Alabama on a U.S. map.]_~~ Her family name was

McCauley. Rosa and her family were African

American. Rosa grew up on a small farm with

her brother, mother, and grandparents.


Rosa was a happy child. She loved her family.

However, Rosa lived at a time and in a place where African Americans faced

discrimination. This was especially true in the South, where Rosa lived,

because in that part of the United States there was **segregation** . ~~_Segregation_~~

~~_was the practice under law of keeping groups of people separate._~~ Because

of segregation, African Americans and white people did not go to the same

schools, eat at the same restaurants, or go to the same movie theaters. When

traveling by bus, African Americans were expected to sit in certain seats. It

was as if African Americans and white people lived in different worlds.


**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen actively, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses;
**TEKS 2.6.A** Establish purpose for reading assigned and self-selected texts; **TEKS 2.6.E** Make connections to personal
experiences, ideas in other texts, and society; **TEKS 2.6.F** Make inferences and use evidence to support understanding;
**TEKS 2.7.F** Respond using newly acquired vocabulary as appropriate.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


**Show Image 9A-2: Example of segregation in**
**the South**

When Rosa was a little girl, she attended a

school that was just for African American

children. It was an old, one-room schoolhouse

that only held classes for five months of each

year. Far too often, there weren’t enough desks

or school supplies for the students. Rosa

noticed that buses took white children to the

new school near where she lived.


When Rosa was eleven years old, she was sent to Montgomery, Alabama, to

continue her studies. But when she was sixteen, Rosa had to leave school to

care for her grandmother and her mother, who had both become ill. To help

support the family, Rosa worked in a shirt factory.


**Show Image 9A-3: NAACP activists,**
**including Thurgood Marshall**

When Rosa was nineteen, she married

Raymond Parks. Raymond was a barber. ~~_What_~~

~~_do you think a barber does?_~~ He was also

actively involved in the Montgomery chapter of

the National Association for the Advancement

of Colored People (NAACP). ~~_The word_~~ ~~chapter~~

~~_here means a group in a certain area that makes up one small section of a_~~

~~_larger group._~~


~~_This image shows another chapter of the NAACP from another state._~~ This

organization wanted to make life in the United States fairer and safer for

all African Americans and end segregation in the South. Rosa also became

involved and served as the Montgomery chapter secretary. In addition, she

returned to high school to earn her high school diploma.


Then came the day in Rosa’s life when she stood up for what was right.

Actually, Rosa did not stand up, and that’s the reason why we remember and

honor Rosa Parks to this day.



Support


The word _chapter_ can
also refer to one of the
main sections of a book.


Challenge


Have students predict
what the author
means by the sentence
“Actually, Rosa did not
stand up, and that’s
the reason why we
remember and honor
Rosa Parks to this day.”



Lesson 9 Rosa Parks: The Mother of the Civil Rights Movement
**135**


**136**



**Show Image 9A-4: Rosa boarding the bus**

The evening of December 1, 1955, in

Montgomery, Alabama, was cold. The streets

were full of people shopping or making their

way home from work. By this time, Rosa had

a job as a seamstress at a local department

store. Her day had just ended, and she had

rushed to catch the city bus that would take

her home. ~~_[Ask students to locate Rosa in the line waiting for the bus. (in the_~~

~~_green coat and hat) Ask them to locate Rosa on the bus when you turn to image_~~

~~_9A-5.]_~~ As Rosa boarded the bus, she could see that it was already quite full.

Because the section at the back of the bus where African Americans usually

sat was so full, Rosa found a seat near the middle of the bus.


**Show Image 9A-5: Rosa sitting on the bus**

The seat Rosa found was just behind the seats

reserved for white people. Before long, all the

seats on the bus were full, and several white

people were left standing. Back then, the bus

driver had the authority, or power, to move

people, and African Americans were the first to

be moved.


The bus driver noticed the people standing and ordered several African

American people on the bus to give up their seats. All of them did as he

asked except for Rosa. When the bus driver told Rosa that if she did not

stand up, he would call the police to come and arrest her, she quietly

responded, “You may do that.” When he asked her one more time to stand up,

Rosa responded by saying, “I don’t think I should have to stand up.” ~~_Why do_~~

~~_you think Rosa refused to stand up?_~~


**Show Image 9A-6: Rosa being fingerprinted**

The bus driver made the call, and before long,

a police officer arrived. The officer wanted to

know why she would not give up her seat. Rosa

responded by asking the officer a question.

“Why are you always pushing us around?” she

asked. Rosa was arrested and taken to the

police headquarters. Later that night, she was

released on bail. ~~_Bail is the money someone pays when he or she has been_~~

~~_arrested so he or she can stay out of jail until the trial or hearing._~~


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


The law at that time in Alabama gave bus drivers the right to **assign** seats.

~~_The word_~~ ~~assign~~ ~~_means to give or to place._~~ As a result of the consequences

that would come from refusing to give up their seats, many African American

people felt threatened and were frightened to challenge this practice. But

Rosa had done so. ~~_What does it mean to challenge something?_~~


Rosa later said that she had not planned to protest, but in that moment,

her desire for civil rights and her sense of **injustice** drove her to make that

decision. ~~Injustice~~ ~~_means a lack of fairness._~~ “When I made that decision,”

Rosa said, “I knew that I had the strength of my ancestors with me.”


This was the moment that changed history. Rosa had refused to move, and

now others would show their support for her.


**Show Image 9A-7: Crowds of people walked**
**to work**

It was decided that the hundreds of African

Americans who rode the city buses to work

would walk instead. This kind of action is

called a **boycott** . ~~_A boycott is when a group_~~

~~_of people join together and refuse to do_~~

~~_business with a company or an organization._~~

~~_In Montgomery, people refused to ride the city buses._~~ The NAACP began

to organize what became known as the Montgomery Bus Boycott. African

American women’s groups began to organize, too.


**Show Image 9A-8: Rosa and Martin Luther**
**King Jr.**

A young man named Dr. Martin Luther King

Jr. led the boycott. Dr. King said, “We will

walk until justice runs down like water and

righteousness like a mighty stream.” Beginning

on December 5, 1955, people refused to ride

the city buses in Montgomery. The boycott

lasted for 382 days. ~~_One year has 365 days in it. If the boycott lasted 382_~~

~~_days, was it shorter or longer than a year?_~~ Without passengers to ride

the buses, the buses couldn’t afford to run. At that time in Montgomery,

more than seven out of every ten riders on buses were African American.

Businesses were **disrupted** . ~~_When something is disrupted, it means that_~~

~~_certain things are prevented from happening due to possible confusion._~~ Many

white people supported the boycott, too.



Challenge


Have students explain
the meaning of the
phrase “the strength of
my ancestors with me.”



Lesson 9 Rosa Parks: The Mother of the Civil Rights Movement
**137**


**138**



On November 13, 1956, the United States Supreme Court decided that

Montgomery’s segregated bus seating was unconstitutional. That meant

that it was against the law. A court order was served on December 20, 1956,

and the boycott ended the next day. After that, buses were integrated, and

African American people could sit wherever they wanted. That boycott

became a powerful way for people to peacefully protest. ~~_When people protest_~~

~~_peacefully, they do not use violence._~~


**Show Image 9A-9: Map with Michigan and**
**Alabama highlighted**

Rosa Parks’s actions helped grow the civil

rights movement. In fact, Rosa became

known as the “mother of the civil rights

movement.” ~~_What is the civil rights movement?_~~

Rosa remained an active member of the NAACP

and other civil rights groups. She showed her

support for Dr. Martin Luther King Jr. by participating in civil rights marches.


Rosa became a member of the staff of a Michigan congressman. ~~_The state of_~~

~~_Michigan is highlighted in blue in this image, and Alabama, where Rosa rode_~~

~~_the bus, is highlighted in green._~~ She worked in his office for twenty-three

years—from 1965 until she retired in 1988. Rosa also founded an institution

to help young people complete their education.


**Show Image 9A-10: Rosa receiving the Medal**
**of Freedom**

In her lifetime, Rosa received several awards

for her courage and her work. In 1979,

the NAACP awarded Rosa its Spingarn

Medal. Rosa also received two of the U.S.

government’s most important civilian honors,

or those honors given to people who aren’t

serving in our military. Rosa received the Presidential Medal of Freedom in

1996 and the Congressional Gold Medal of Honor in 1999.


Rosa once said, “I’d see the bus pass every day. But to me, that was a way

of life; we had no choice but to accept what was the custom. The bus was

among the first ways I realized there was a black world and a white world.”

Incredibly, by her own actions, Rosa Parks changed that world. Rosa became

a symbol of the power of nonviolence. ~~_What does_~~ ~~nonviolence~~ ~~_mean?_~~ Her

quiet, courageous act changed America and changed the course of history.


Unit 10 Fighting for a Cause


~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


Check for Understanding


**Evaluate:** What is the connection between a bus and Rosa Parks’s
fight for civil rights? _(On a bus, Rosa Parks refused to give up her_
_seat simply because she was African American. She used this form of_
_nonviolent protest to fight for civil rights.)_


**1. Inferential.** Why did Rosa Parks refuse to give up her seat? _(Rosa knew that_
_the segregation laws were unfair and chose to fight to end segregation.)_

   - **Literal** What are some examples of discrimination that Rosa Parks faced?
_(segregated schools that were open for fewer months of the year than_
_white schools; segregation in public places such as schools and places of_
_employment; segregation on buses; etc.)_


**2. Literal.** What did many people do to support Rosa Parks? _(People refused_
_to ride the city buses. They organized a boycott that became known as the_
_Montgomery Bus Boycott.)_


**3. Inferential.** How are Rosa’s actions connected to the civil rights movement?
_(Rosa’s actions are connected to the civil rights movement in that she_
_protested against discrimination on the city buses and demanded laws treat_
_people equally.)_

   - **Literal.** What is the civil rights movement? _(The civil rights movement is_
_the series of events in America in the 1950s and 1960s focused on ensuring_
_people were treated equally under the law regardless of race.)_


**4. Literal.** Who organized the Montgomery Bus Boycott? _(Martin Luther King_
_Jr.)_ Was the boycott successful? _(yes)_ How do you know? _(Segregation on_
_buses was outlawed.)_


**5. Literal.** How did Rosa Parks continue helping others after the Montgomery
Bus Boycott? _(Rosa supported Martin Luther King Jr.; she worked in a_
_congressman’s office; she founded an institute to help young people complete_
_their education.)_


**6. Evaluative.** _Think-Pair-Share:_ How is the United States today different than
the United States of Rosa Parks’s early life? _(Answers may vary, but may_
_include that today there is no segregation on buses or in schools, and there is_
_less discrimination.)_



Support


Review the meaning of
the word _discrimination_ .
